Milk Dominance in Fosterage: When Mixtures Establish Prohibition
Ruling on milk mixed with medicine for fosterage

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi condition of milk dominance (ghalabah) to determine whether a mixture of milk and another substance establishes fosterage, and cite the hadith proof.

Core Idea
Only when milk is the dominant ingredient does a mixture satisfy the hunger condition for fosterage.

The Hanafi school bases fosterage on the milk's ability to satisfy hunger, as derived from the hadith 'la rada' illa ma shadd al-'azm wa anbat al-lahm.' When milk is mixed with medicine, the key question is whether the milk remains the primary nutritional component. This lesson examines the criterion of dominance (ghalabah) and its application to such mixtures.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Ruling Scenario
The passage presents a case where a woman's milk is mixed with medicine and fed to a child within the first two years. The question is whether this creates a fosterage relationship that prohibits marriage.
The two-year limit is a Hanafi condition for fosterage.
2
Determine the Criterion: Milk Dominance
The text explicitly states that if the milk is dominant (ghalib), the prohibition is attached. If the medicine is dominant, no fosterage arises.
Dominance means the milk constitutes the majority of the mixture and effectively satisfies hunger.
3
Trace the Underlying Principle
The passage explains that the basis is whether the milk acts as the origin (asl) that relieves hunger. This comes from the hadith 'al-rada' min al-maja’ah' (fosterage is from hunger).
The physical effect of strengthening bone and growing flesh is the evidence that hunger has been satisfied.
4
Link to the Hadith Proof
The hadith from Ibn Mas'ud (reported in Abu Dawud and Dar Qutni) states: 'No fosterage except what strengthens bone and grows flesh.' This indicates that milk must be the primary source of nutrition for fosterage to take effect.
The hadith is ahad (zanni al-thubut), but accepted in the Hanafi school.
5
Contrast with Subsidiary Consumption
If the milk is consumed as a subsidiary (tabi')—for example, if the mixture is mostly medicine and the milk merely adds taste—then the condition of satisfying hunger is not met, and no fosterage is established.
The general rule: all such issues turn on whether milk is primary (asl) or subsidiary (tabi').

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
واذا اختلط بالدواء واللبن غالب تعلق به التحريم [۱۸۷۵] (۱۵) اگر دودھ پل جائے دوا کے ساتھ اور دودھ غالب ہو تو حرمت اس سے متعلق ہوگی۔ کسی عورت کے دودھ کو دوا کے ساتھ ملا کر دو سال کے اندر بچے کو پلایا اور دودھ غالب ہو تو بچے کی رضاعت اس عورت سے ثابت ہو (۱) دودھ غالب ہے اس لئے دودھ اصل ہو گیا اور بھوک دور کرنا ثابت ہو گیا اس لئے حرمت ثابت ہو جائے گی۔ ول ان سب مسئلوں کا دارو مدار اس بات پر ہے کہ دودھ اصل بن کر بھوک دور کر رہا ہو تو اس سے حرمت رضاعت ثابت ہوگی۔ اور اگر تابع بن کر پیا جار ہا ہو تو حرمت رضاعت ثابت نہیں ہوگی۔ اور اس کی دلیل حدیث کا لفظ الرضاعة من المجاعة ہے (۲) عن ابن مسعود قال لا رضاع الامـا شـد العظم وانبت اللحم (الف ) ( ابو داؤد شریف ، باب رضاعتہ الکبیر ص ۲۸۸ نمبر ۲۰۵۹ ردار قطنی ، کتاب الرضاع ج رابع ص ۱۰۲نمبر ۴۳۱۵)
And if it mixes with medicine and the milk is dominant, prohibition is attached to it. [1875] (15) If milk is fed with medicine and the milk is dominant, then prohibition will be attached to it. A woman's milk mixed with medicine and fed to a child within two years, and the milk is dominant, then the child's fosterage from that woman is established. (1) The milk is dominant, so the milk becomes the origin and hunger-satiating is established, so prohibition is established. And the basis for all these issues is that if milk acts as origin and satisfies hunger, then fosterage prohibition is established. And if it is drunk as subsidiary, then fosterage prohibition is not established. And its proof is the hadith phrase 'al-rada' min al-maja'ah' (fosterage is from hunger) (2) From Ibn Mas'ud, he said: No fosterage except what strengthens bone and grows flesh. (Abu Dawud Sharif, Bab Rada'at al-Kabir, p. 288 no. 2059; Dar Qutni, Kitab al-Rada', vol. 4 p. 102 no. 4315)
Source pages p. 86

Commentary
This passage presents a ruling from Kitab al-Rada' concerning when a woman's milk is mixed with another substance—here, medicine—and fed to a child. The key principle is that fosterage (rada') is based on the milk being the primary substance that satisfies hunger. If the milk is dominant (ghalib) in the mixture, it is considered the origin (asl) and thus establishes the prohibition of marriage between the child and the woman (and her relatives). If the milk is not dominant, no prohibition arises. The commentary explains that this ruling is derived from the hadith 'al-rada' min al-maja'ah' (fosterage is from hunger) and the statement of Ibn Mas'ud that fosterage only counts if it strengthens bone and grows flesh. The hadith indicates that the criterion is the milk itself acting as food that nourishes and sustains. Therefore, when milk is mixed with medicine but remains the predominant ingredient, it still fulfills the condition of satisfying hunger, and thus the ruling of fosterage applies. The passage also sets a general rule: all such issues turn on whether the milk is consumed as the primary substance (asl) or as a subsidiary (tabi'). Only when it is primary does it create the fosterage relationship.

Key Terms
رضاع (rada'): Fosterage relationship created by breastfeeding within the first two years that makes marriage prohibited between the child and the nursing woman and certain relatives.
غالب (ghalib): Dominant; refers to the milk being the majority or primary component in a mixture such that it is the effective substance in satisfying hunger.
مجاعة (maja'ah): Hunger; the hadith 'al-rada' min al-maja'ah' indicates that fosterage is based on the milk relieving hunger.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
When a woman's milk is mixed with medicine and fed to a child, under what condition does the prohibition of marriage (fosterage) become established according to the Hanafi school?
A) If the medicine is dominant
B) If the milk is dominant
C) If the child is under one year old
D) If the woman had previously breastfed the child

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school requires milk to be the dominant component in any mixture for it to establish fosterage, based on the principle that fosterage is tied to satisfying hunger.

Milk Mixture: Dominance Determines Fosterage Prohibition
Ruling on milk mixture for fosterage

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on milk mixtures for fosterage and apply the principle of dominance (ghalabah) to determine if the prohibition is established.

Core Idea
Only when human milk is dominant in a mixture with animal milk does it create a fosterage prohibition.

In Hanafi fiqh, fosterage (radha'ah) is established when a child is fed human milk under specific conditions. A key scenario is when human milk is mixed with animal milk. This passage explains that the mixture is treated as human milk only if the human component is dominant (ghalib). This lesson unpacks that ruling and its legal reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The act is feeding a child a mixture of human and animal milk. The ruling is that fosterage prohibition is attached only if the human milk is dominant.
2
Define 'dominant' (ghalib) in Hanafi law
Dominance is quantitative: the component that constitutes more than half of the mixture. The mixture is then attributed to that component.
3
Apply the dominance test
If human milk is more than half, the mixture is considered human milk. If animal milk is more than half, it is considered animal milk.
Equality (exactly half) is not dominance; the mixture does not count as human milk.
4
Determine the legal effect
If human milk is dominant, feeding the mixture establishes fosterage and the associated marriage prohibitions. If not, no prohibition arises.
5
Note the basis
The foundation is the hadith that fosterage is established by milk that 'strengthens bone and grows flesh.' This requires the milk to be the primary nourishment, which is true only if human milk is the dominant component.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
واذا اختلط لبن المرأة بلبن شاة ولبن المرأة هو الغالب تعلق به بالتحريم فان غلب لبن الشاة لم يتعلق به التحريم ۱۸۷۶) (۱۷) اگر مل گیا دودھ بکری کے دودھ کے ساتھ اور وہ غالب ہے تو متعلق ہوگی اس سے حرمت۔ پس اگر غالب ہو گیا بکری کا ددھ تو اس سے حرمت متعلق نہیں ہوگی۔ عورت کا دودھ بکری کے دودھ کے ساتھ ملا دیا اور عورت کا دودھ غالب ہو اور اس کو کسی بچے کو پلا دے تو اس سے حرمت رضاعت ثابت ہو جائے گی۔ اور اگر عورت کا دودھ مغلوب ہو تو حرمت رضاعت ثابت نہیں ہوگی۔ عورت کا دودھ غالب ہو تو وہ بھوک دور کرنے میں اصل ہو گیا اس لئے اس سے حرمت رضاعت ثابت ہوگی ۔ اصول اور اس کے لئے حدیث پہلے گزر چکی ہے۔
And if a woman's milk mixes with goat's milk and the woman's milk is dominant, the prohibition is attached to it; if the goat's milk is dominant, the prohibition is not attached to it. 1876) (17) If milk is mixed with goat's milk and it (woman's milk) is dominant, then prohibition will be attached to it. If goat's milk becomes dominant, then prohibition is not attached to it. A woman's milk is mixed with goat's milk and the woman's milk is dominant and it is fed to a child, then fosterage prohibition will be established. If the woman's milk is dominated, fosterage prohibition is not established. When the woman's milk is dominant, it becomes the origin in satisfying hunger, therefore fosterage prohibition is established. The principle and hadith for this have passed earlier.
Source pages p. 86

Commentary
This passage discusses the case where a woman's milk is mixed with goat's milk. The ruling is that if the woman's milk is the dominant (ghalib) component, then the mixture is considered as human milk for the purpose of fosterage, and marriage becomes prohibited. Conversely, if the goat's milk is dominant, the mixture is treat as animal milk and does not create a fosterage relationship. The rationale is that only when human milk is the primary substance that satisfies hunger (the 'origin' in providing nourishment) does the underlying principle apply—that fosterage is based on milk that strengthens bone and grows flesh (as per the hadith mentioned earlier). The goat's milk, even if present, does not have the human aspect, so it cannot establish the human fosterage relationship unless it is the lesser component. This illustrates the consistent Hanafi criterion: dominance (ghalabah) determines whether the milk functions as the effective substance for fosterage.

Key Terms
غالب (ghalib): Dominant; the component in a mixture that is greater in quantity (more than half).
مغلوب (maghlub): Dominated; the component in a mixture that is lesser in quantity and does not affect the ruling.
رضاعت (radha'ah): Fosterage; a legal relationship established by breastfeeding that creates marriage prohibitions.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If a woman's milk is mixed with goat's milk and fed to a child, under which circumstance does fosterage become established?
A) If the goat's milk is dominant
B) If the woman's milk is dominant
C) If the child is under one year
D) Fosterage is never established with animal milk present

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school requires the human milk component to be dominant in a mixture with animal milk for fosterage prohibition to be established.

Milk of Two Women Mixed: Dispute on Establishing Fosterage
Intra-Hanafi dispute on fosterage from mixed milk

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the intra-Hanafi dispute on fosterage from mixed milk and explain the reasoning of Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani.

Core Idea
When two women's milk mixes, only the majority establishes fosterage per Abu Yusuf, but both do per Muhammad — a dispute over origin vs subsidiary.

This passage presents a fine intra-Hanafi legal dispute: if milk from two women is mixed and fed to a child, does the child become a mahram to both women or only to the one whose milk is greater in quantity? The answer hinges on whether one woman's milk can be considered the 'origin' and the other a 'subsidiary'. Understanding this dispute sharpens your ability to apply the principles of istihsan and qiyas within the school.

Guided Path
1
Identify the case
Recognize the scenario: milk from two women is mixed and fed to a child. The legal question is whether fosterage prohibition is established with one or both women.
Fosterage (rida') creates a permanent marriage prohibition.
2
State the ruling in the matn
The matn (base text) says: 'the prohibition is attached to the majority (bi-aktharihima).' This is the starting point.
The matn is the concise statement that the commentary expands.
3
Explain Abu Yusuf's view
Abu Yusuf argues that the majority milk becomes the origin (asl) because it satisfies hunger; the minority milk is subsidiary (tabi'). Only the origin creates prohibition.
This view applies the principle that the dominant component determines the ruling.
4
Explain Muhammad's view
Muhammad al-Shaybani holds that since both milks are of the same genus (human milk), the concept of origin and subsidiary does not apply. Both are origins, so both women become foster mothers.
This view emphasizes the nature of the substance over quantitative dominance.
5
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf
Practical consequence: Under Abu Yusuf, only the woman with more milk is a foster mother; under Muhammad, both are. This affects whom the child may marry.
The mufti should be aware of both positions and choose the operative one.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
واذا اختلط لبن امرأتين يتعلق به التحريم باكثرهما ۱۸۷۷) (۱۸) اگر دو عورتوں کا دودھ ملا دیا تو حرمت متعلق ہوگی ان دونوں میں سے اکثر کے ساتھ امام ابو یوسف کے نزدیک ۔ اور امام محمد عند ابي يوسف رحمه الله تعالى وقال محمد رحمه الله تعالى تعلق بهما التحرتيم [۱۷] (على ما في النص) نے فرمایا حرمت متعلق ہوگی دونوں کے ساتھ۔ امام ابو یوسف فرماتے ہیں کہ جس عورت کا دودھ زیادہ ہے بھوک دور کرنے میں وہ اصل ہو گیا اور دوسرا تابع ہو گیا اس لئے جس عورت کا دودھ زیادہ ہو اس سے حرمت رضاعت ثابت ہوگی۔ امام محمد قفرماتے ہیں کہ دونوں ایک ہی جنس ہیں اس لئے اصل اور تابع کا اعتبار نہیں ہوگا۔ بلکہ دونوں اصل ہوں گے۔اس لئے دونوں عورتوں سے حرمت رضاعت ثابت ہوگی ۔
And if the milk of two women mixes, the prohibition is attached to the larger of them. 1877) (18) If the milk of two women is mixed together, then prohibition will be attached to the larger of the two according to Imam Abu Yusuf. And Imam Muhammad, said: 'prohibition is attached to both.' Abu Yusuf says: whichever woman's milk is more, in satisfying hunger it became the origin and the other became subsidiary, therefore fosterage prohibition is established from the woman with more milk. Imam Muhammad says: both are of the same genus, so origin and subsidiary cannot be considered; rather both are origins, therefore fosterage prohibition is established from both women.
Source pages p. 86

Commentary
This passage presents an intra-Hanafi dispute regarding the case where the milk of two women is mixed together and fed to a child. The ruling in the matn (text) states that prohibition is attached to the majority (bi-aktharihima). Imam Abu Yusuf interprets this to mean that only the woman whose milk constitutes the greater portion is considered the source of fosterage; the other woman's milk is subsidiary (tabi') and does not establish a relationship. Imam Muhammad, however, holds that since both milks are of the same genus (both human), the concept of one being origin and the other subsidiary does not apply. Instead, both women are considered origins (asl), and thus the child becomes a mahram to both women through fosterage. This difference stems from the principle of whether a subsidiary (tabi') can carry the same ruling as the origin when the substances are identical. The relied-upon position (mufta bihi) in the Hanafi school is often the view of Imam Muhammad, but this passage presents both opinions.

Key Terms
اکثر (akthar): Greater in quantity; the majority component in a mixture.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Imam Abu Yusuf, when milk from two women is mixed and fed to a child, with whom is the fosterage prohibition established?
A) With both women equally
B) Only with the woman whose milk is greater in quantity
C) With neither woman
D) With the woman who breastfeeds first

Key Takeaway
The dispute on mixed milk fosterage hinges on whether the minority milk is subsidiary or independent; the mufta bihi is typically Muhammad's view that both women become foster mothers.

Milk from a Virgin Woman Establishes Fosterage
Ruling on fosterage from virgin's milk

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on fosterage from a virgin woman's milk and explain the Quranic proof that supports it.

Core Idea
A virgin's milk creates fosterage prohibition because the Quran calls any nursing woman a 'mother.'

This lesson addresses a specific question: does milk from a virgin woman (who has never had intercourse) establish the same fosterage prohibition as milk from a woman who has given birth? The source text provides a clear ruling and cites a Quranic verse. We will examine the proof and the reasoning behind the Hanafi position.

Guided Path
1
Identify the subject and ruling
The subject is a virgin woman (bikr) who produces milk—whether naturally or due to medication—and breastfeeds a child. The ruling is that fosterage prohibition is established.
The term 'bikr' is defined as a woman who has not had intercourse.
2
Locate the Quranic proof
The proof is from Surah al-Nisa 4:23, which says 'And your mothers who have breastfed you.' The word 'mothers' is general.
The verse establishes prohibition from nursing mothers.
3
Analyze the wajh al-dalalah (point of evidence)
The verse does not restrict 'mothers' to those who have given birth. Therefore, any woman who breastfeeds, including a virgin, is included. Hence, the prohibition applies.
The key is the act of breastfeeding, not the cause of milk production.
4
Note the cross-madhhab difference
Some schools require that milk be produced as a result of pregnancy for fosterage to be established. The Hanafi school includes milk from any cause.
This is a legitimate ikhtilaf.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
واذا نزل للبكر لبن فارضعت صبيا يتعلق به التحريم [۱۸۷۸) (۱۹) اگر با کرہ عورت کو دودھ اترے اور کسی بچے کو پلا دیا تو اس سے حرمت متعلق ہو گی۔ انی عورت کو دودھ دوطرح سے اترتا ہے۔ ایک بچہ پیدا ہونے کے بعد اور دوسرا کچھ دوائی کھانے سے۔ اس صورت میں صحبت کئے بغیر بھی عورت سے دودھ اتر سکتا ہے۔ چونکہ عورت سے دودھ اتر رہا ہے اس لئے اس کا حکم بھی وہی ہے جو بچہ پیدا ہونے کے بعد دودھ اترے۔ یعنی اس کے پینے سے بھی حرمت رضاعت ثابت ہوگی۔ آیت میں ہے و امهاتكم التي ارضعنكم ( آیت ۲۳ سورۃ النساء ۴) آیت میں پلانے والی ماں سے حرمت کا ثبوت ہے۔ اور یہ بھی پلانے والی ماں ہے اس لئے اس کے پلانے سے بھی حرمت ثابت ہوگی۔ البكر : وہ عورت جس سے صحبت نہ ہوئی ہو۔
And if milk descends for a virgin woman and she breastfeeds a child, prohibition is attached to it. [1878] (19) If milk descends to a virgin woman and she feeds a child, then prohibition will be attached to it. Milk descends to a woman in two ways: one after childbirth, and the other from consuming some medicine. In this case, milk can descend without intercourse. Since milk is descending from the woman, its ruling is the same as when milk descends after childbirth. That is, drinking it establishes fosterage prohibition. In the verse: 'And your mothers who have breastfed you' (Surah al-Nisa 4:23), the verse establishes prohibition from the nursing mother. And she too is a nursing mother, so her nursing also establishes prohibition. 'Al-bikr': a woman who has not engaged in intercourse.
Source pages p. 87

Commentary
This passage addresses the case of a virgin woman (bikr) who produces milk, whether through pregnancy or due to medicinal causes (such as hormone-inducing drugs). The ruling is clear: if she breastfeeds a child, the fosterage prohibition is established. The reasoning is based on the Quranic verse: 'And your mothers who have breastfed you' (4:23). The verse uses the word 'mothers' (ummahaat) which includes any woman who breastfeeds, not only those who have given birth. Since the virgin woman is indeed breastfeeding, she qualifies as a 'mother' in this context. The mode of milk production (through childbirth or medicine) is irrelevant; the key is that she is nursing the child. The commentary also defines 'bikr' as a woman who has not had intercourse. This ruling emphasizes that the act of breastfeeding itself is the determining factor, not the woman's marital status or whether she has ever been pregnant.

Key Terms
بکر (bikr): A woman who has never engaged in sexual intercourse, regardless of age.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, if a virgin woman (who has never had intercourse) produces milk and breastfeeds a child, what is the ruling?
A) Fosterage is not established because she is not a mother.
B) Fosterage is established because she is nursing the child.
C) Fosterage is only established if the milk is from pregnancy.
D) Fosterage is established only if she is married.

Key Takeaway
The act of breastfeeding itself, regardless of the woman's marital history or the cause of milk, establishes fosterage prohibition.

Milk from a man does not create fosterage prohibition
Ruling on milk from a man and fosterage

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on a man's milk producing fosterage and explain why the Quranic restriction to 'mothers' is the basis.

Core Idea
Fosterage is maternal; a man's milk has no legal effect on prohibition.

Fosterage (rida') creates permanent marriage prohibitions, but only when certain conditions are met. This lesson addresses a rare but important edge case: a man who produces milk and breastfeeds a child. The Hanafi school, relying on the Quranic text and early practice, firmly rules that such milk does not establish any prohibition. Understanding this clarifies that fosterage is inherently tied to the female role, not merely the act of feeding.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The text states: if a man's milk descends and he feeds a child, no prohibition is attached. Identify the act (breastfeeding by a man) and the ruling (no prohibition).
2
Locate the primary dalil
The text cites Quran 4:23: 'Your mothers who have breastfed you.' Identify the key word 'mothers' (ummahaat) and note its implication.
3
Analyze the wajh al-dalalah
The term 'mothers' is restricted to females. The absence of any mention of males proves that the cause of fosterage is the female, not merely the act of feeding.
4
Examine the secondary evidence
The athar from Jabir and 'Amir (in Musannaf Ibn Abi Shaybah) confirms that early authorities did not consider a man's milk (laban al-fahl) as effective for fosterage.
Despite the hadith being mursal and zanni, it supports the Quranic inference.
5
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf
Consider the practical implication: if another school held differently, what would change? The Hanafi position avoids creating parent-child relationships from a man's milk.
Even if the man is the biological father, no additional prohibition arises.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

Commentary
This passage addresses the rare case of a man producing milk and breastfeeding a child. The ruling is clear: such milk does not establish the fosterage prohibition. The reasoning is based on the Quranic verse 'Your mothers who have breastfed you' (4:23), which restricts the fosterage relationship to women. The word 'mothers' (ummahaat) limits the subject to females. Since the milk comes from a man, he cannot be considered a 'mother', and thus no prohibition arises. Additionally, the athar (narrative) from Jabir and 'Amir confirms that the early authorities did not give any legal effect to a man's milk. The commentary also notes that the milk is 'directly from the father' (baap ka doodh), emphasizing that even if a man lactates, it does not create a foster relationship. This ruling underscores that fosterage is a maternal institution, tied to the female who nurses.

Key Terms
فحل (fahl): Literally 'stallion'; here it refers to a man (male) who produces milk.
رضاعة (rada'ah): Fosterage; the legal relationship created when a woman breastfeeds a child under certain conditions, establishing permanent marriage prohibitions.

Milk from a man does not establish fosterage; the proof is the Quran's restriction to 'mothers' and the early authorities' practice.

Fosterage is Exclusive to Human Milk
Ruling on two children drinking from the same goat's milk

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on fosterage from animal milk and explain the Quranic proof that restricts fosterage to human mothers.

Core Idea
Fosterage (rida'ah) is established only through human milk; animal milk creates no legal relationship.

This lesson examines a specific case: two children drinking from the same goat's milk. Does this make them foster siblings? The Hanafi school answers with a clear 'no', grounded in the Quranic definition of breastfeeding. Understanding this ruling clarifies the boundaries of fosterage law.

Guided Path
1
Identify the legal issue
The issue is whether drinking animal milk establishes fosterage (rida'ah) between children.
2
State the Hanafi ruling
No fosterage is established; the children are not foster siblings.
3
Locate the Quranic proof
The verse 4:23: 'And your mothers who have breastfed you' (Surah al-Nisa).
The proof is a nas (text) from the Quran.
4
Analyze the linguistic evidence (wajh al-dalalah)
The term 'mothers' (ummahaat) is specific to human females. A goat is not a mother, so the verse does not apply.
The dalala (indication) is zanni because the word could be interpreted literally; the Hanafi take it as exclusive.
5
Confirm the operative position
This is the mufta bihi position of the Hanafi school, with no disagreement from the Sahibayn.
Most other schools agree.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
[۱۸۸۰] (۲۱) اگر دو بچوں نے بکرنی کا دودھ پیا تو دونوں کے درمیان رضاعت نہیں ہے۔ رضاعت انسانی اعضاء کی جزئیت سے ہوتی ہے۔ اور یہ حیوان کا دودھ ہے اس لئے اس سے رضاعت نہیں ہوگی (۲) آیت میں امهات کہا ہے کہ ماں کا دودھ پلائے تب رضاعت ہوگی اور بکری ماں نہیں ہوگی اس لئے اس کے دودھ پینے سے رضاعت نہیں ہوگی (۳) بلکہ انسان
[1880] (21) If two children drink from a goat's milk, there is no fosterage between them. Fosterage occurs from the part of human body parts. And this is animal milk, so fosterage does not occur. (2) In the verse, it says 'mothers' that when a mother breastfeeds then fosterage occurs, and a goat is not a mother, so drinking its milk does not create fosterage. (3) Rather human...
Source pages p. 87

Commentary
This passage addresses a scenario where two children both drink from the same goat's milk. The ruling is that this does not establish any fosterage relationship between the children (i.e., they are not considered foster siblings). The reasons given are: (1) fosterage is specifically related to human body parts (i.e., human milk from a nursing mother), so animal milk is not included. (2) The Quranic verse 'And your mothers who have breastfed you' (4:23) uses the word 'mothers' (ummahaat), which refers to human mothers. A goat is not a mother in the legal sense, so its milk cannot create the fosterage tie. (3) The commentary begins stating 'Rather human...' but is cut off, likely intending to say that only human milk creates this relationship. The underlying principle is that fosterage is a legal relationship derived from human breastfeeding, and animal milk does not share the same human element.

Key Terms
بکرنی (bakrni): A female goat (nanny goat).
رضاعت (rida'ah): Fosterage; the legal relationship created by breastfeeding.

Ruling on Milk from a Man: No Fosterage Prohibition
States that milk from a man does not establish fosterage prohibition

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why the Hanafi school rules that a man's milk does not create fosterage prohibition, citing the Quranic proof and the Companions' athar.

Core Idea
A man's milk does not establish fosterage prohibition because the Quranic text restricts nursing to mothers (women).

This lesson examines a clear ruling in Hanafi fiqh: a man who produces milk and feeds an infant does not create the marriage prohibitions that fosterage entails. The proof is drawn from the Quranic verse 'your mothers who nursed you' and a reported effect from the Companions. Understanding this distinction is essential for correctly applying the rules of rada'ah.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The act is a man feeding an infant with his milk. Determine the ruling: does it establish fosterage prohibition?
The ruling is that no prohibition is established.
2
Locate the primary dalil
Find the Quranic proof. The verse is Surah al-Nisa 4:23: '...your mothers who nursed you...'.
The operative word is 'mothers' (ummahat).
3
Analyze the wajh al-dalalah
Examine how the verse proves the ruling. 'Mothers' linguistically denotes females, so nursing is exclusive to women. A man is not a mother, so his milk is not included.
The text is clear (nass) and cannot be extended to males.
4
Confirm with secondary dalil
The athar of Jabir and 'Amir shows that they did not consider the milk of a male (al-fahl) to be anything, confirming the Hanafi position.
This strengthens the proof with Sunnah (Companion statement).
5
Note cross-madhhab status
While Hanafi and majority schools agree, some schools (e.g., Zahiri) may differ. Recognize the ruling is muttafaq 'alayh among the four schools.
The ikhtilaf is with a minority position.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
[۱۸۷۹) (۲۰) اگر مرد سے دودھ اتر جائے اور اس کو کسی بچے کو پلا دے تو اس سے حرمت متعلق نہیں ہوگی۔

آیت میں امهاتكم التي ارضعنكم کہا ہے کہ ماں دودھ پلائے تو حرمت ثابت ہوگی۔ اور یہ دودھ براہ راست باپ کا ہوگا اس لئے اس سے حرمت ثابت نہیں ہوگی۔

اثر میں ہے۔ عن جابر عن عامر انهما كانا لايريان لبن الفحل شيئا (الف)(مصنف ابن ابی شيبة ۱۸۷ من رخص في لبن المحل ولم یر و هیاج رابع میں ۱۹ نمبر ۱۷۳۵۶) اس اثر سے ثابت ہوا کہ مرد کے دودھ سے حرمت رضاعت نہیں ہوگی۔
[1879] (20) If milk descends from a man and he feeds it to an infant, then prohibition does not attach. The verse says 'your mothers who nursed you', meaning it is the mother who nurses and thus prohibition is established. And this milk would be directly from the father, therefore prohibition is not established from it. There is an athar (tradition) from Jabir from 'Amir that both of them did not consider the milk of a male (al-fahl) to be anything (Musannaf Ibn Abi Shaybah, 187, chapter on concession regarding the milk of a husband, and they did not see...). This athar proves that from a man's milk, fosterage prohibition does not occur.
Source pages p. 87

Commentary
This ruling states that if milk descends from a man and he breastfeeds an infant, fosterage prohibition (tahrim rada'ah) does not take effect. The proof is twofold: first, the Quranic verse 'your mothers who nursed you' (4:23) specifies the mother, i.e., a woman, as the nursing party; a man is not a mother. Second, a reported effect (athar) from the Companions Jabir and 'Amir indicates that they did not consider the milk of a male (al-fahl) to establish any prohibition. Thus, even if a man produces milk, it does not create the marriage prohibitions that fosterage entails. This is a clear ruling in the Hanafi school.

Key Terms
الفحل (al-fahl): Literally 'stallion' or 'male'; in the context of fosterage, refers to a man who produces milk.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

C) The verse 'And your mothers who nursed you' from Surah al-Hajj
D) The verse 'Mothers shall nurse their children' from Surah al-Baqarah

Key Takeaway
Milk from a man does not establish fosterage prohibition because the Quranic and Sunnah evidence restricts the nursing relationship to women.

Goat's Milk and Fosterage: Only Human Milk Counts
States that goat's milk does not create fosterage

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling that fosterage (radā‘) is established only with human milk and apply this ruling to determine marriage prohibitions.

Core Idea
Fosterage in Hanafi law requires human breast milk; animal milk never creates legal ties.

This passage from a classical Hanafi text delivers a crisp principle: goat's milk does not create fosterage. It then illustrates the consequences of valid fosterage through a concrete scenario. Understanding this distinction is key to correctly applying marriage prohibitions in the Hanafi school.

Guided Path
1
Identify the ruling and its scope
The first phrase, 'لبن شاة فلا رضاع بينهما', unequivocally states that goat's milk does not create fosterage. This tells us that radā‘ is limited to human milk.
The term 'shāt' (goat) is used as an example; the ruling applies to all animal milk.
2
Trace the implication to the scenario
The passage then presents a case: if an older wife breastfeeds a younger wife with her own human milk, both wives become forbidden to the husband. This follows the principle that valid fosterage (human milk) prohibits marriage.
The scenario assumes the older wife’s milk is human—otherwise the ruling would be different.
3
Analyse the legal consequences for mahr
If the husband had not consummated with the older wife, she forfeits her mahr because she caused the marriage to be void. The younger wife receives half the mahr, and the husband may recover from the older wife if she acted intentionally.
This shows how mahr is adjusted when a marriage is invalidated due to fosterage.
4
Note the procedural rule on testimony
The passage ends by stating that in fosterage cases, women’s testimony alone is not accepted—corroboration is required. This highlights the evidentiary standards for proving fosterage.
This is a procedural rule specific to radā‘ cases.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
لبن شاة فلا رضاع بينهما [۱۸۸۱] (۲۲) واذا تزوج الرجل صغيرة وكبيرة فارضعت الكبيرة الصغيرة حرمتا على الزوج [ ۱۸۸۲ (۲۳) فان كان لم يدخل بالكبيرة فلا مهر لها وللصغيرة نصف المهر [۱۸۸۳ (۲۳) ويرجع به الزوج على الكبيرة ان كانت تعمدت به الفساد وان لم تتعمد فلا شيء عليها [ ۱۸۸۴ (۲۵) ولا تقبل في الرضاع شهادة النساء منفردات

(23) If he had not consummated with the older woman, then she has no mahr, and for the young girl half the mahr. (24) And the husband recovers it from the older woman if she intentionally caused the corruption; if she did not intend, then nothing is upon her. (25) And testimony in fosterage of women alone is not accepted.
Source pages p. 88

Commentary
This passage is a ruling statement: For fosterage (radā‘) to be established, the milk must be from a human mother. If a goat's milk is fed, no fosterage relationship arises because the milk is not from a human woman. This is a basic Hanafi principle: radā‘ is defined by human breast milk within the period of infancy (before two years). The passage then states a series of rulings that are consequences of this principle, but the first sentence is a standalone ruling: goat's milk does not count.

Key Terms
رضاع (radā‘): Fosterage relationship established when a woman breastfeeds a child under two years with her milk, creating certain marriage prohibitions.
لبن شاة (laban shāt): Goat's milk; used in the text as an example of animal milk that does not create fosterage.
مهر (mahr): Dower or bridal gift given by the husband to the wife as part of the marriage contract.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Key Takeaway

Freeing a Mukatab Without Payment for Zihar Expiation
Ruling with proof on freeing a mukatab for expiation

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on freeing a mukatab who has not paid anything for zihar expiation and explain the legal rationale based on the mukatab's status.

Core Idea
A mukatab who has paid nothing is a full slave, so freeing him fulfills expiation.

This lesson examines a specific case in zihar expiation: freeing a mukatab who has made no payment under his kitaba contract. The text clarifies that such a mukatab retains full slave status, making his emancipation valid for kaffarah. Understanding this ruling hinges on the Hanafi principle that a mukatab remains a slave until full payment.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The act is freeing a mukatab (a slave under kitaba contract) who has not paid anything. The ruling is that it is valid (ja'iz) for zihar expiation.
2
Understand the status of the mukatab
The commentator explains that because no payment has been made, there is 'no trace of freedom' and the mukatab is a 'complete slave.' Thus freeing him counts as freeing a full slave.
Contrast this with a mukatab who has paid part of the kitaba; he would be partially free and not a full slave.
3
Trace the evidence (dalil)
The text references a hadith (from Abu Huraira, mentioned later) that supports this ruling. The hadith is ahad and has zanni thubut and dalala, but it is sufficient for the Hanafi position.
The hadith indicates that a mukatab who has not paid is still a slave.
4
Note the ikhtilaf
Some scholars (non-Hanafi) consider a mukatab partially free even before payment. The Hanafi school rejects this view, maintaining full slave status until full payment.
This ikhtilaf has practical implications: only the Hanafi position allows freeing a mukatab without payment for expiation.
5
Confirm the mufta bihi
The ruling in the text (and from Zahir al-Riwayah) is the mufta bihi position. It is relied upon for fatwa.

Source Text

If he frees a mukatab who has not paid anything, it is valid. And if he freed a mukatab who had not paid anything, it is valid. Since he has not paid anything from the kitaba, there is no trace of freedom in him, so he is a complete slave. Therefore freeing him for expiation is valid. What happens from merely making a contract of kitaba? (2) The above hadith also supports this.

Commentary
This passage states a ruling: a mukatab (a slave who has made a contract of kitaba to earn his freedom) is considered a full slave for purposes of expiation if he has not yet paid anything towards his kitaba. The commentator explains that because no payment has been made, the mukatab retains full slave status, so freeing him fulfills the requirement of freeing a slave for kaffarah. The ruling is supported by a hadith (mentioned later).

Key Terms
مكاتب (mukatab): A slave who has made a contract (kitaba) with his master to earn his freedom by paying installments.
كتابة (kitaba): A contract between a master and slave whereby the slave earns freedom by paying a sum in installments.

Buying a Mahram Relative for Expiation: Ruling and Proof
Ruling on buying a mahram relative and intending expiation

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on buying a mahram relative for expiation and explain why the intention makes the purchase count as freeing a slave.

Core Idea
Intention transforms automatic freedom into valid expiation.

This lesson examines a specific scenario where a person purchases a mahram relative intending that purchase to serve as the freeing of a slave for zihar expiation. The Hanafi school, based on the hadith of Samurah ibn Jundub, holds that upon purchase, the relative becomes free immediately due to the mahram rule, and simultaneously, the buyer's intention is recognized, thus fulfilling the expiation. We will explore the proof and the legal reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
What is the act being ruled upon? Buying a father, son, or any mahram relative with the specific intention that this purchase counts as the freeing of a slave for expiation. The ruling is that it is valid (jaza) and the expiation is fulfilled.
The act is a purchase with dual legal effect.
2
Locate the primary evidence
The Hanafi school relies on the hadith of Samurah ibn Jundub: 'Whoever owns a mahram relative, he becomes free.' This establishes immediate emancipation upon acquiring ownership of a mahram.
This is an ahad hadith, zanni al-thubut, but accepted in Hanafi jurisprudence.
3
Understand the role of intention
Although the hadith alone would cause automatic freedom, the buyer's intention for expiation is also legally effective. The text states that because the buyer intended expiation, that purpose is considered, and the expiation is valid.
Two causes of freedom coexist: mahram ownership and expiation freeing. Intention tips the scale for expiation.
4
Consider the cross-madhhab perspective
Some schools (e.g., Shafi'i) may argue that automatic freedom precludes the purchase from serving a separate purpose. The Hanafi view emphasizes that the hadith does not negate intention, and thus the expiation is fulfilled.
The practical consequence: in the Hanafi school, one does not need a separate act of freeing; the purchase suffices.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فان اشتری اباه اوابنه وينوى بالشراء الكفارة جاز عنها. اگر اپنی باپ، بیٹے بازی رحم محرم کو خریدنے سے نیت کی کفارے کی تو کفارے سے کافی ہوگا۔ باپ، بیٹے بازی رحم محرم کو خریدنے سے پہلے کفارے کی نیت تھی تو خریدتے ہی آزاد ہو جائیں گے لیکن کفارہ بھی ادا ہو جائے گا۔ یہاں آزاد ہونے کے دو اسباب ہیں۔ ایک ذی رحم محرم ہونے کی وجہ سے آزاد ہونا اور دوسرا کفارے کی وجہ سے آزاد ہونا۔ چونکہ خریدنے والی کی نیت کفارہ کی جانب سے آزاد کرنا ہے اس لئے اس کی رعایت ہوگی اور کفارہ ادا ہو جائے گا۔
If he buys his father or his son and intends by the purchase expiation, it is valid for him. If he buys his father, son, or mahram relative intending expiation, it will suffice. He intended expiation before buying, so upon purchase they become free and the expiation is fulfilled. There are two causes of freedom: being a mahram relative and being freed for expiation. Since the buyer intended expiation, that is considered and the expiation is valid.
Source pages p. 164

Commentary
This ruling covers a scenario where a person buys his father, son, or any mahram relative with the intention that this purchase counts as the freeing of a slave for zihar expiation. The commentator explains that upon purchase, the relative becomes free immediately due to the hadith 'Whoever owns a mahram relative, he becomes free.' Additionally, the buyer's intention for expiation is recognized, so the expiation is fulfilled. Two causes of freedom overlap here, but the intention determines the expiation.

Key Terms
ذو رحم محرم (dhu rahim mahram): A relative whom one cannot marry due to blood relationship (e.g., father, son, brother).
شراء (shira'): Purchase, buying.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

B) The father becomes free and the expiation is valid because intention matters.
C) The father remains a slave until explicitly freed.
D) The expiation is only valid if the father is bought from a third party.

Key Takeaway
Intention can make a coincidental automatic freedom count as expiation in Hanafi fiqh.

Freeing Half of a Shared Slave and Guaranteeing the Rest
Intra-Hanafi dispute on partial manumission for expiation

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the intra-Hanafi dispute on freeing a share of a jointly-owned slave for expiation and explain why the Sahibayn qualify the ruling based on the manumitter's wealth.

Core Idea
Freeing half of a jointly-owned slave for expiation is only valid if the manumitter is wealthy—otherwise the share's inherent defect renders the act incomplete.

This passage examines a disagreement within the Hanafi school on whether freeing a share of a slave and then guaranteeing and freeing the remainder can fulfill expiation. The difference hinges on the legal effect of partial manumission and the manumitter's financial status. The Sahibayn introduce a qualification that Abu Hanifa does not accept, and the text grounds their reasoning in a Prophetic hadith and two underlying principles.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and the ruling
The manumitter frees half of a jointly-owned slave for expiation, then guarantees the value of the co-owner's share and frees that half as well. Abu Hanifa rules this act invalid for expiation; the Sahibayn rule it valid only if the manumitter is wealthy.
The act is a single process: partial freeing + guarantee + second freeing.
2
Understand the Sahibayn's position and its condition
If the manumitter is wealthy (mūsir), the guarantee immediately completes the slave's freedom, making the entire slave free in a valid act. If poor (mu‘sir), only the freed half is free, and the expiation is incomplete.
Wealth is the key condition for validity in their view.
3
Locate the two underlying principles (qawa‘id)
First principle: from the hadith of Abu Huraira, partial manumission leads to full freedom if the manumitter is wealthy, or to the slave earning the remaining value if poor. Second principle: whether the co-owner's share is freed with a defect (nuqs) or fully and immediately.
Abu Hanifa emphasizes the defect; the Sahibayn emphasize the guarantee's effect.
4
Trace the rationale of each imam
Abu Hanifa: The first half was freed with partial freedom, a defect. When the second half is later freed, the whole is not freed in a single valid act, so expiation fails. Sahibayn: The guarantee prevents the defect; if wealthy, the whole becomes free instantly; if poor, the defect remains.
They agree the act is invalid if the manumitter is poor.
5
Evaluate the thamarah al-khilaf and tarjih
Practical difference: If a wealthy man frees his share and guarantees, the Sahibayn count it as full expiation; Abu Hanifa does not. Later Hanafi works adopt the Sahibayn's view as mufta bihi.
The mufta bihi position is that the expiation is valid if the manumitter is wealthy.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وان اعتق نصف عبد مشترک عن الكفارة وضمن قيمة باقيه فاعتقه لم يجز عند ابي حنيفة رحمه الله وقال ابويوسف و محمد رحمهما الله يجزيه ان كان المعتق موسرا وان كان معسرا لم يجز. اگر مشترک غلام کے آدھے کو آزاد کیا کفارے کی طرف سے اور ضامن ہو گیا باقی کی قیمت کا پھر اس کو آزاد کیا تو کافی نہیں ہے امام ابو حنیفہ کے نزدیک۔ اور فرمایا صاحبین نے کہ کافی ہوگا اگر آزاد کرنے والا مالدار ہے۔ اور اگر تنگدست ہے تو کافی نہیں ہوگا۔ یہ مسئلہ دو قاعدوں پر ہے۔ ایک قاعدہ یہ ہے کہ غلام دو آدمیوں کے درمیان مشترک ہو اور ایک آدمی اپنا حصہ آزاد کرے تو اگر وہ مالدار ہے تو پورا غلام ہی آزاد ہو جائے گا اور شریک کے حصے کی قیمت ادا کرنی ہوگی۔ اور اگر آزاد کرنے والا غریب ہے تو جتنا اس نے آزاد کیا اتنا آزاد ہوگا اور باقی حصے کا غلام کما کر آقا کو ادا کرے گا پھر آزاد ہوگا۔ اس قاعدے کی دلیل یہ حدیث ہے۔ عن ابي هريرة ان النبي الله قال من اعتق نصيبا او شقيصا فی مملوک فخلاصه عليه في ماله ان كان له مال والا قوم عليه فاستسعی به غير مشقوق عليه۔ اور دوسرا قاعدہ یہ ہے کہ شریک کا حصہ جو آزاد ہوا اس میں نقص آکر آزاد ہوا یا مکمل آزاد ہوا تو امام ابو حنیفہ فرماتے ہیں کہ دوسرے کے حصے میں پہلے آزادگی کا نقص آیا پھر اس کا ضامن ہوا پھر آزاد ہوا اس لئے نقص والا غلام آزاد ہوا لکمل غلام آزاد نہیں ہوا۔ اس لئے یہ غلام کفارے کے لئے کافی نہیں ہے۔ اور صاحبین فرماتے ہیں کہ آزاد کرنے والے نے شریک کی ذمہ داری لے لی تو شریک کے حصے میں کمی نہیں آئی ۔ بلکہ مکمل غلام آزاد ہوا۔ اس لئے کفارہ کے لئے کافی ہے۔ اور اگر آزاد کرنے والا تنگدست ہو تو آدھا غلام ہی کفارے والے کی جانب سے آزاد ہوا باقی آدھے کے بارے میں غلام خود سعی کر کے رقم ادا کرے گا اور آزاد ہوگا اس لئے کفارہ ادا نہیں ہوگا۔
If he frees half of a shared slave for expiation and guarantees the value of the remainder and then frees it, it is not valid according to Abu Hanifa. Abu Yusuf and Muhammad said it is valid if the manumitter is wealthy, and if he is poor it is not valid. The text explains the two principles behind this.
Source pages p. 164

Commentary
This passage presents an intra-Hanafi dispute on freeing a half-share of a jointly-owned slave for zihar expiation, with a guarantee to free the other half. Abu Hanifa holds that the expiation is not fulfilled because the process involves a defect (nuqs) in the slave's freedom; the slave was only partially freed initially, then the guarantee and subsequent freeing do not make the entire slave free in a single valid act. The Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) differentiate based on the wealth of the manumitter: if wealthy, the guarantee makes the whole slave free immediately, so expiation is valid; if poor, only the half is freed and the expiation is not fulfilled. The text supports this with the hadith of Abu Huraira regarding freeing a share and with the rule of 'isti'ana' (seeking earnings) for the poor manumitter.

Key Terms
نصيبا او شقيصا (nasiban aw shaqisan): A share or portion of a jointly-owned slave.
موسر / معسر (mūsir / mu'sir): Wealthy / poor; determines liability for the co-owner's share.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Sahibayn, when is freeing half of a shared slave and then guaranteeing and freeing the rest valid for expiation?
A) Only if the manumitter is poor.
B) Only if the manumitter is wealthy.
C) Always valid regardless of wealth.
D) Never valid.

Key Takeaway
Freeing half of a jointly-owned slave for expiation is valid only if the manumitter is wealthy, according to the relied-upon Hanafi position (Sahibayn).

Freeing Half of One's Own Slave Then the Rest for Expiation
Ruling on freeing half then the rest of one's own slave for expiation

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the ruling on sequentially freeing one's own slave for expiation and explain why it is valid based on the principle of sole ownership.

Core Idea
Freeing half of your own slave then the rest counts as a complete expiation because you own the whole slave.

In the previous lesson, we saw that freeing a share in a jointly-owned slave invalidates partial expiation. Here, the ruling changes when the slave is wholly owned. This passage clarifies that the defect of partial freedom is not considered because the manumitter is the sole owner.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and the ruling
What action is described? The person frees half of his own slave for his expiation, then later frees the remaining half. What is the ruling? The text says 'jāza' (it is valid).
2
Understand the underlying principle
Why is it valid? Because the slave is wholly owned by the manumitter. The initial half-freeing does not create a legal defect; it is as if the entire slave was freed in one continuous act.
The principle: ownership eliminates the issue of partial freedom.
3
Contrast with joint ownership
Recall the case of a jointly-owned slave. There, freeing one's share alone is not sufficient for expiation because the partner's share remains unfreed. Here, no partner exists, so the entire slave is fully freed over time.
This contrast highlights the importance of sole ownership.
4
Apply to new scenarios
If a person owns multiple slaves, can he free parts of different slaves? No, each expiation requires freeing one complete slave. But the sequential freeing of the same slave is valid.
The ruling is specific to one's own single slave.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وان اعتق نصف عبده عن كفارته ثم اعتق باقيه عنها جاز. اور اگر اپنے ہی غلام کے آدھے حصے کو کفارے کی طرف سے آزاد کیا پھر باقی کو اس کی جانب سے آزاد کیا تو جائز ہے۔ اشی کفارے والے نے اپنے غلام کے آدھے حصے کو آزاد کیا پھر باقی آدھے حصے کو بعد میں آزاد کیا تو کفارہ کی طرف سے کافی ہوگا۔ آدھے غلام کو آزاد کرنے سے جو نقص ہوا وہ اپنی ملکیت میں ہوا اس لئے مکمل غلام آزاد کرنا سمجھا جائے گا اور ایسا ہوا کہ ایک کفارہ دو جملوں میں ادا کیا اس لئے کافی ہوگا۔ اصول یہ مسئلہ اس اصول پر ہے کہ اپنا آدھا غلام آزاد کرے اور باقی کو آزاد کرے تو اس نقص کا اعتبار نہیں ہے۔ کیونکہ مالک ہونے کی وجہ سے گویا کہ پورا غلام ایک مرتبہ ہی آزاد ہوا۔
If he frees half of his slave for his expiation, then frees the rest for it, it is valid. And if a person freed half of his own slave for expiation and then freed the remaining half later, it is valid. The defect that occurred from freeing half was in his own ownership, so the entire slave is considered freed, and it is as if one expiation was performed in two installments, so it is valid. The principle is that when one frees half of his own slave and then the rest, the defect is not considered because by virtue of ownership, it is as if the whole slave was freed at once.
Source pages p. 165

Commentary
This ruling clarifies that if a person frees half of his own slave (not jointly owned) for zihar expiation, and later frees the remaining half, the expiation is valid. The key difference from the previous case is that the slave is wholly owned by the manumitter. The initial half-freeing does not create a defect that invalidates the expiation because the manumitter is the sole owner; the entire slave is freed effectively in one continuous act. The principle is that ownership eliminates the issue of partial freedom affecting validity.

Key Terms
نصف عبده (nisf 'abdihi): Half of his own slave (i.e., he owns the whole slave).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Because the slave is jointly owned.
B) Because the defect from partial freedom is not considered when the owner is the sole owner.
C) Because the expiation requires two separate acts.
D) Because the slave must be freed entirely at once.

Key Takeaway
Freeing one's own slave in two installments for expiation is valid because sole ownership removes the defect of partial freedom.

Freeing Half a Slave for Zihar Expiation: Sequence Matters
Ruling on freeing half of slave, then intercourse, then freeing rest for expiation

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the ruling when partial manumission is followed by intercourse before completing expiation, and identify the sequence requirement from Quranic evidence.

Core Idea
Freeing a slave in parts is allowed, but for zihar expiation, the entire slave must be freed before intercourse.

This lesson examines a specific scenario in zihar expiation: a man frees half his slave, then has intercourse with his wife, then frees the other half. According to Imam Abu Hanifa, this does not fulfill the expiation. Understanding the sequence is crucial: the expiation must be completed before any marital relations resume. The ruling underscores the strict condition that the kaffarah must be fully discharged prior to intercourse.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and ruling
The act is freeing half a slave for zihar expiation, then intercourse, then freeing the rest. The ruling according to Abu Hanifa is that the expiation is not valid (batil).
2
Locate the dalil
The Quranic verse on zihar (58:3-4) requires freeing a slave before intercourse. This verse is the primary evidence.
3
Trace the wajh al-dalalah
The verse implies that the full expiation must precede intercourse; partial freeing does not meet the condition. The word 'then' ( ثُمَّ ) in the verse indicates sequence.
The commentary in the source references the verse's implication.
4
Understand the Hanafi principle of tajri
Hanafis allow freeing a slave in parts (tajri), but here it does not help because the sequence is broken. The hadith about freeing a share supports tajri, but the sequence condition overrides.
The hadith is from Ibn Umar about freeing a share.
5
Note the consequence
The expiation is not valid; the man must start over by freeing a full slave before intercourse.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وان اعتق نصف عبده عن كفارته ثم جامع التى ظاهر منها ثم اعتق باقيه لم يُجز عند ابي حنيفة. اور اگر اپنے غلام کا آدھا اپنے کفارے کی طرف سے آزاد کیا پھر ولی کی اس عورت سے جس سے ظہار کیا تھا پھر آزاد کیا باقی غلام کو تو امام ابو حنیفہ کے نزدیک کافی نہیں ہوگا۔ آیت کے اعتبار سے ولی سے پہلے پورا غلام کفارہ میں ادا کرنا چاہئے ۔ اس نے آدھا غلام ہی ادا کیا اور آدھا بعد میں ادا کیا۔ اور حنفیہ کے نزدیک غلام آزاد کرنے میں تجری ہو سکتی ہے اس لئے آدھا ہی آزاد ہوا اس لئے کفارے میں کافی نہیں ہے۔ حدیث سے معلوم ہوتا ہے کہ غلام آزاد کرنے میں تجری ہوسکتی ہے۔ حدیث میں ہے۔ عن ابن عمر قال قال رسول الله . اعتق شركا له فى عبد فكان له مال يبلغ ثمن العبد قوم عليه قيمة العدل فاعطى شركاء حصصهم وعتق عليه العبد
If he frees half of his slave for his expiation, then has intercourse with the woman from whom he made zihar, then frees the rest, it is not valid according to Abu Hanifa. And if he freed half of his slave for expiation, then had intercourse with the woman from whom he had made zihar, then freed the rest, it is not sufficient according to Imam Abu Hanifa. According to the verse, before intercourse, the entire slave must be given in expiation. He only gave half and the other half later. And according to the Hanafis, freeing a slave can be done in parts, so only half was freed, thus it is not sufficient for expiation. The hadith indicates that freeing a slave can be done in parts. The hadith is from Ibn Umar: The Prophet said, 'Whoever frees a share in a slave and has wealth sufficient to cover the full price, the slave is valued fairly, and he gives the partners their shares, and the slave becomes free.'
Source pages p. 165

Commentary
This ruling addresses a case where a man frees half of his slave for zihar expiation, then engages in intercourse with his wife (which would be forbidden before complete expiation), and then frees the other half. According to Abu Hanifa, this does not fulfill the expiation because once intercourse occurs before the full expiation, the sin is incurred and the subsequent freeing does not rectify the sequence. The expiation must be completed before intercourse. The text references a hadith about partial freeing, but the key point is that the sequence matters: intercourse before full expiation invalidates the partial freeing as expiation.

Key Terms
تجری (tajrī): The permissibility of freeing a slave in parts (gradual manumission).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

B) The expiation is not valid because intercourse occurred before full expiation.
C) The expiation is valid only if the manumitter is wealthy.
D) The expiation is valid because freeing in parts is allowed.

Key Takeaway
When using a slave for zihar expiation, the entire slave must be freed before any marital relations resume; partial freeing followed by intercourse invalidates the expiation.

Minimum Duration of Pregnancy: Six Months
Establishes minimum pregnancy using Quranic proof

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to derive the Hanafi ruling on the minimum pregnancy period using Quranic verses and explain its application in a case of disputed lineage.

Core Idea
The Quran sets total pregnancy and weaning at 30 months; weaning is 24 months, so minimum pregnancy is 6 months.

Understanding the minimum duration of pregnancy is crucial in matters of lineage, inheritance, and criminal accusations. This lesson examines the Hanafi position, derived from a combination of two Quranic verses and supported by an athar of Ali ibn Abi Talib.

Guided Path
1
Identify the ruling
The Hanafi school holds that the minimum duration of pregnancy is six lunar months.
2
Locate the primary dalil
Two Quranic verses are combined: Surah Luqman 31:14 (weaning period of two full years = 24 months) and Surah Ahqaf 46:15 (total of pregnancy and weaning = 30 months).
The verses are not directly quoted in full but referenced in the athar.
3
Understand the arithmetic derivation
Subtract 24 months (weaning) from 30 months (total) to get 6 months as the minimum pregnancy.
This is a straightforward mathematical deduction from the combined verses.
4
Apply the supporting athar
The athar of Ali confirms the ruling: when Umar intended to stone a woman who gave birth at six months, Ali cited these verses to show the pregnancy is possible and no punishment is due.
This athar demonstrates the practical application of the derivation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
بعد : ختم ہونے کے بعد حمل ٹھہرا ہو (۲) اثر میں ہے کہ عمل کی کم سے کم مدت چھ ماہ ہیں۔ ان عمر اتى بامرأة قد ولدت لستة اشهر فهم برجمها فبلغ ذلك عليا فقال ليس عليها رجم فبلغ ذلك عمر فارسل اليه فسأله فقال والوالدات يرضعن اولادهن حولين كاملين لمن اراد ان يتم الرضاعة وقال تعالى وحمله وفصاله ثلاثون شهرا، فستة اشهر حمله وحولين تمام لاحـد عـلـيـهـا او قال لا رجم عليها فخلى عنها ثم ولدت (الف)
Meaning: After the completion (of iddah) the pregnancy occurred. (2) There is an athar that the minimum period of pregnancy is six months. Umar brought a woman who gave birth at six months and intended to stone her. That reached Ali who said there is no stoning on her. Umar sent for him and asked, so Ali said: 'And mothers shall nurse their children for two complete years for whoever wants to complete the nursing' and Allah says 'and his carrying and weaning is thirty months.' So six months for pregnancy and two years complete for weaning. He said: there is no hadd on her, or he said no stoning, so he released her then she gave birth. (Reference).
Source pages p. 204

Commentary
This passage establishes the minimum period of pregnancy as six months, based on a combination of two Quranic verses. The first verse (Surah Luqman, 31:14) states that weaning occurs in two years (24 months). The second verse (Surah Ahqaf, 46:15) states that the total period of pregnancy and weaning is 30 months. Subtracting the two years of weaning leaves six months as the minimum pregnancy. The athar of Ali confirms this interpretation: when Umar wanted to stone a woman who gave birth at six months, Ali cited these verses to show that such a birth is possible and no punishment applies. Therefore, the Hanafi school upholds that the minimum pregnancy period is six lunar months.

Key Terms
حمل (haml): pregnancy
أقل الحمل (aqall al-haml): minimum duration of pregnancy
أثر (athar): a report from a Companion (here, Ali ibn Abi Talib)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) 4 months
B) 6 months
C) 9 months
D) 12 months

Key Takeaway
The minimum pregnancy period in Hanafi fiqh is six lunar months, derived from Quran 31:14 and 46:15.

Male 'Asabat's Right to Custody When No Female Custodian Exists
Matn and commentary on male agnates' priority in custody

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on male 'asabat's custody priority in the absence of female custodians and explain the evidence from the hadith of Hamzah's daughter.

Core Idea
When no female custodian exists, the nearest male agnate takes custody.

This passage from al-Sharh al-Saghir addresses a specific custody scenario: when no female relative is available to care for a child, which male relative has the strongest claim? The matn establishes the principle that the nearest 'asabah has priority, and the commentary illustrates it with a prophetic decision. By studying this lesson, you will understand how the Hanafi school applies the rules of 'asabah to custody disputes.

Guided Path
1
Identify the legal scenario
Read the matn: 'If there is not for the boy a woman from his family, and the men dispute over him...' This sets the condition for the ruling.
The ruling only applies when no female custodian (mother, grandmother, etc.) exists.
2
State the ruling
The matn states: 'then the most entitled among them is the nearest in 'asabah.' This is the Hanafi position.
'Asabah' refers to male agnates related through the paternal line.
3
Locate the dalil
The commentator cites the hadith of Hamzah's daughter: Ali, Ja'far, and Zayd (all 'asabah) claimed custody, but the Prophet gave her to the maternal aunt.
The hadith shows that 'asabah have a claim, but a female relative (maternal aunt) takes precedence when present.
4
Analyze the wajh al-dalalah
The Prophet's decision to give custody to the maternal aunt indicates that in the presence of a female relative, men do not get priority. Conversely, when no woman exists, the 'asabah are entitled, and among them the closest in 'asabah wins.
The hadith also confirms that 'asabah are suitable claimants.
5
Note the cross-madhhab difference
The Shafi'i school prioritizes the father over other male relatives, while the Hanafi school prioritizes the nearest 'asabah (which could be a grandfather, brother, uncle, etc. after the father).
This difference has practical implications in custody disputes.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فان لم تكن للصبى امرأة من أهله فاختصم فيه الرجال فأولاهم به أقربهم تعصيبا. قال علي ان احق بها وهي ابنة عمي وقال جعفر ابنة عمي وخالتها تحتي وقال زيد ابنة احي فقضى بها النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم لخالتها
If there is not for the boy a woman from his family, and the men dispute over him, then the most entitled among them is the nearest in 'asabah. Ali said: I am more entitled to her, and she is the daughter of my paternal uncle. Ja'far said: (She is) the daughter of my paternal uncle, and her maternal aunt is under my marriage. Zayd said: (She is) the daughter of my brother. Then the Prophet (peace be upon him) decided in favor of her maternal aunt.
Source pages p. 227

Commentary
This passage states the ruling regarding custody when there is no female custodian available. The matn establishes that male agnates ('asabat) have the right to custody, and priority among them is determined by proximity in the 'asabah line, following the order of inheritance. The commentator illustrates this with the well-known incident of Hamzah's daughter: Ali (ibn Abi Talib), Ja'far, and Zayd all claimed custody. The Prophet (peace be upon him) gave custody to the maternal aunt (who was married to Ja'far), indicating that in the absence of women, the 'asabah have entitlement, but the maternal aunt (as a female relative) if available may take precedence. However, the ruling in the matn specifically addresses when there is no woman at all. The mother's right to custody if she marries a stranger is discussed separately earlier in the text (not in this passage). The key point: among men, the closest 'asabah to the child gets custody.

Key Terms
عصبة ('asabah): Male agnates related through the paternal line who inherit by 'asabah.
حضانة (hadanah): The right to physically care for and raise a child.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, when there is no female relative available for custody, who among the men has the strongest right to custody?
A) The closest male agnate ('asabah) based on inheritance priority
B) The father regardless of other relatives
C) The maternal uncle
D) The judge chooses arbitrarily

Key Takeaway
In the absence of female custodians, the Hanafi school grants custody to the nearest male agnate ('asabah) in the order of inheritance.

Maintenance of Poor Parents and Grandparents: Obligation and Proofs
Ruling with multiple proofs on obligation to maintain poor parents and grandparents

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on maintenance of poor parents and grandparents, identify the Quranic and hadith proofs, and apply the ruling to cases where parents are non-Muslim or wealthy.

Core Idea
Maintenance of poor parents (and grandparents) is wajib on the son, regardless of religion.

This passage establishes a fundamental Hanafi ruling: a man must financially support his parents, grandparents, and grandmothers if they are poor, even if they are non-Muslim. The author cites multiple Quranic verses and hadith to prove this obligation. Understanding this ruling requires analyzing the proofs and their implications, including the condition of poverty and the cross-madhhab disagreement.

Guided Path
1
Identify the subject and ruling
Pinpoint exactly who must provide maintenance (the son/man) and to whom (parents, grandparents, grandmothers) under what condition (if they are poor). Note that the ruling states it is obligatory (lazim/wajib).
The text says 'aur aadmi par lazim hai' – it is obligatory upon the man.
2
Examine the Quranic proofs
Two verses are cited: 1) Quran 31:15 commands kind treatment without any condition of faith, so maintenance (a form of kindness) is due. 2) Quran 2:233 states that the heir has a similar obligation – the son is an heir of his parents.
The verses are quoted partially in Arabic; note the absence of a religion condition.
3
Examine the hadith proofs
Two hadith are presented: one from Abu Dawud where the Prophet (ﷺ) mentions parents first in the order of kindness; another from Nasa'i where the Prophet says to start with those whom you support: mother, father, sister, brother. This establishes priority and obligation.
Hadith: 'Your mother, your father... this is an obligatory right.'
4
Note the condition of poverty
The ruling is conditional: maintenance is wajib only if the parents/grandparents are poor (have no daily food) and the son has means. If they have their own wealth, their maintenance is from their own wealth, not on the son.
This condition is explicit: 'jab woh faqeer hon' – when they are poor.
5
Note inclusion of grandparents and cross-madhhab difference
Grandparents and maternal grandparents are included by analogy (they are ascendants and heirs). Some Shafi'i scholars disagree regarding non-Muslim parents, arguing that since they do not inherit, maintenance is not obligatory. The Hanafi position relies on the unconditional command in Quran 31:15.
The Hanafi view is that the Quranic command is general; no condition of Islam is made.

Therefore it is not permissible to take the child outside the city without the husband's permission. The verse is: 'No parent shall be harmed because of her child, nor any father because of his child' (al-Baqarah 2:233). However, the place where the husband married the wife is her maternal home, there is her family, so she will go there, and when she goes herself, she has the right to take the child with her. Otherwise, the mother would be harmed. And the previous verse said that the mother should not be harmed because of the child. So she has the right to take him to her maternal home. [2175] (48) And it is obligatory upon the man to spend on his parents and grandfathers and grandmothers if they are poor, even if they oppose him in religion. If the parents do not have daily food and the son has, then providing maintenance to the parents is obligatory on the son, whether he is Muslim or disbeliever. Similarly, the ruling applies to grandfathers, grandmothers, and maternal grandparents. One should treat parents with respect. So if they have no maintenance, he should give them support. (2) The verse says: 'And accompany them in this world with kindness' (Luqman 31:15). This verse says to treat parents well in the world. And because there is no condition of unity of religion, even if parents are disbelievers, treat them well and give them maintenance. (2) Another verse says: 'And upon the heir is like that' (al-Baqarah 2:233) that just as parents have a right to maintenance, so does the heir. And the son and grandson are heirs, so they are also obligated to provide maintenance to parents, grandfathers, grandmothers, and maternal grandparents. (3) In the hadith: Kalib ibn Manfa'ah narrated from his grandfather that he came to the Prophet (ﷺ) and said, 'O Messenger of Allah, whom should I treat with kindness?' He said, 'Your mother, your father, your sister, your brother, and your master who is close to you; this is an obligatory right and a connection of kinship.' (Abu Dawud, Bab fi bir al-walidayn, p. 352 no. 5140) (4) In Nasa'i: From Tariq al-Miharbi, he said: We arrived in Madinah, and there was the Messenger of Allah (ﷺ) standing on the pulpit addressing the people, saying, 'The hand that gives is upper; start with those whom you support: your mother, your father, your sister, your brother, then those closest to you, then closest.' (Nasa'i, Bab istihma' al-yad al-'ulya, p. 350 no. 2533) From this hadith it is also understood that maintenance of parents is obligatory on the son. And grandfathers, grandmothers, and maternal grandparents are included among them, so their maintenance is also obligatory. And if these people have their own wealth, then the son is not obligated to provide maintenance, because every person's maintenance is obligatory from his own wealth. So their maintenance is obligatory from their own wealth. Footnotes: (a) 'Mother and father should not suffer loss because of the child.' (b) 'Live with parents with goodwill in this world.' (c) Kalib ibn Manfa'ah says: O Messenger of Allah, with whom should I be kind? He said: 'Your mother, your father, your sister, your brother, and your master who is close to you; this is an obligatory right and maintenance of kinship ties.' (d) The Prophet (ﷺ) was on the pulpit addressing people and was saying: 'The giving hand is upper; start with those whom you support: your mother, your father, your sister, your brother, then those closer to you, give them.'
Source pages p. 231

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with multiple proofs concerning the obligation of a man to provide maintenance (nafaqah) for his parents, grandparents, and grandmothers (both paternal and maternal) when they are poor, even if they differ in religion. The Hanafi position is that financial support is obligatory on the son for his ascendants, regardless of faith, because the Quranic verses and hadith do not restrict it to Muslims. The commentator cites three proofs: Quran 31:15 which commands kind treatment even to non-Muslim parents, Quran 2:233 which places maintenance on heirs (and children are heirs), and two hadith mention parents first in the order of those to be supported. The ruling also applies to grandfathers, grandmothers, and maternal grandparents. However, if the ascendants have their own wealth, maintenance is not obligatory on the son because one's own wealth suffices. The authority weight is Zahir al-Riwayah as it is a standard ruling in the Hanafi school.

Key Terms
النفقة (al-nafaqah): Financial maintenance covering food, clothing, shelter, and other necessities.
الوالدين (al-walidayn): Both parents (mother and father).
الاجداد والجدات (al-ajdad wa al-jaddat): Grandfathers and grandmothers, both paternal and maternal.

Key Terms from Source Passage

A) No, because they are not Muslims.

C) Only if the son is wealthy.
D) No, because non-Muslim parents are not considered 'heirs'.

Key Takeaway
Maintenance of poor parents and grandparents is wajib on the son, irrespective of their religion, based on unconditional Quranic and hadith commands.

Conditions for Valid Emancipation of a Slave
Defining the conditions for valid emancipation: free, adult, sane owner.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the four conditions for a valid emancipation and identify which party can validly perform it.

Core Idea
A valid emancipation requires a free, adult, sane owner freeing his own slave.

Emancipation is a highly rewarded act in Islam, but it is not valid if the person doing it lacks capacity or ownership. The classical Hanafi text lays out the essential conditions in a concise statement. This lesson unpacks the matn, proofs, and practical implications.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
What is the act being discussed? It is itq (emancipation). The ruling is that it is valid (sahih) when conditions are met, otherwise invalid (batil).
2
List the conditions from the matn
The matn states: emancipation occurs from the free, adult, sane person over his property. So four conditions: emancipator must be free, adult, sane, and own the slave.
The Urdu commentary adds that a slave cannot own another slave, so condition of freedom implies owner cannot be a slave.
3
Examine the proofs
The passage cites multiple proofs: Quran (Surah al-Balad), hadith 'No emancipation except in what you own' (proves ownership), hadith 'The pen is lifted from three' (excludes minors and insane), and athar from 'Uthman (supports invalidity of insane person's actions).
Each proof supports one or more conditions.
4
Note the cross-madhhab difference
Some schools may allow a minor or insane person's emancipation with guardian approval; Hanafi strictly requires adult and sane based on the hadith 'the pen is lifted'.
This is a legitimate ikhtilaf.
5
Apply to a scenario
Given a scenario (e.g., a minor owns a slave and says 'you are free'), check each condition: Is he free? Yes. Adult? No. Therefore emancipation is invalid.
