Book of Emancipation [2190] (1) Emancipation occurs from the free, adult, sane person over his property. [2191] (2) So when he says to his slave or... (Arabic fragment) Necessary note: The meaning of 'itq is freeing. The evidence and virtue of freeing is in this verse: 'And what do you know what the steep path is? It is the freeing of a slave or feeding on a day of severe hunger' (Surah al-Balad, verses 12-14). And in the hadith: Abu Huraira said the Prophet said: 'Any man who frees a Muslim slave, Allah will free for every limb of that slave a limb of him from the Fire' (Bukhari, chapter on freeing and its virtue, p.342, no.2517). From this verse and hadith it is known that one should free male and female slaves and there is reward in it. [2190] (1) Emancipation occurs from a free, adult, sane person over his property. If a person is free, adult, sane, and a male or female slave is in his ownership, then he frees his slave, the slave becomes free. The condition of freedom is placed because a slave does not own anything; whatever he has belongs to his master. For example, if a slave who has been given permission to trade owns a slave and wants to free him, emancipation does not occur. (2) The hadith explains this: From 'Amr ibn Shu'ayb from his father from his grandfather that the Prophet said: 'There is no divorce except in what you own, no emancipation except in what you own, and no sale except in what you own' (Abu Dawud, chapter on divorce before marriage, p.305, no.2190). From this hadith it is known that one cannot free a slave he does not own. And a male slave does not own another slave, so he cannot free. And the emancipation of a child or an insane person is not valid because they lack intellect. (2) In the hadith: From 'Ali from the Prophet: 'The pen is lifted from three: from the sleeper until he wakes, from the child until he becomes adult, and from the insane until he becomes sane' (Abu Dawud, chapter on the insane stealing, p.256, no.4403). This hadith shows that the emancipation of a child and an insane person is not considered. (3) In Bukhari there is a saying of a Companion: 'Uthman said: 'There is no divorce for an insane person nor for a drunkard' (Bukhari, chapter on divorce under coercion, p.793, no.5269). This athar also shows that the divorce and emancipation of a child and an insane person are not considered. And the condition of ownership is proven by the hadith of Abu Dawud that has passed: 'No emancipation except in what you own.' (2191) (2) So if he says to his male or female slave 'You are free' or 'You are freed' or 'I have freed you', then he becomes free. Footnotes: (a) The Prophet said: Any man who frees a Muslim, Allah will free for every limb of his a limb from the Fire. (b) The Prophet said: No divorce occurs except in what you own, no emancipation except in what you own, no sale except in what you own. (c) The Prophet said: The pen is lifted from three: from the sleeper until he wakes, from the child until he becomes adult, and from the insane until he becomes sane. (d) 'Uthman said: Divorce is not valid for an insane person or a drunkard.
Source pages p. 240

Commentary
This passage from Kitab al-'Itaq (Book of Emancipation) states the conditions for a valid emancipation (itq). The matn (original text) says: 'Emancipation occurs from the free, adult, sane person over his property.' The commentary explains that the emancipator must be free (not a slave), adult (baligh), sane ('aqil), and must own the slave being freed. The proof includes Quranic verses (Surah al-Balad 90:12-14) and several hadith. The hadith 'No emancipation except in what you own' establishes ownership as a condition. The hadith 'The pen is lifted from three' excludes minors and the insane. The athar from 'Uthman further supports that an insane person's actions are not valid. The commentary also clarifies that a slave cannot emancipate another slave because he does not own him. Additionally, if a person says specific phrases like 'you are free' or 'I have freed you', the slave becomes free immediately.

Key Terms
عتق (itq): Emancipation or freeing of a slave; the act of releasing a slave from bondage.
حر (hurr): Free person (not a slave); a slave cannot emancipate another slave.
بالغ (baligh): Adult who has reached puberty; a minor's emancipation is invalid.
عاقل (aqil): Sane person of sound mind; insane person's actions are not valid.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Which of the following is NOT a condition for valid emancipation according to this passage?
A) The emancipator must be free.
B) The emancipator must be an adult.
C) The emancipator must be the slave's owner.
D) The emancipator must be Muslim.

Key Takeaway
The validity of emancipation hinges on the capacity of the owner: free, adult, sane, and owner of the slave.

Conditions for Valid Emancipation (al-'Itq) in Hanafi Fiqh
Ruling statement with multiple proof citations

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the four conditions for a valid emancipation and identify the proofs from Quran and Sunnah that support each condition.

Core Idea
Emancipation is valid only when the owner is free, adult, sane, and owns the slave in his property.

This lesson opens the Book of Emancipation (Kitab al-'Itaq). The text first establishes the virtue of freeing slaves, then lays down the essential conditions for a valid emancipation, supported by explicit proofs from the Quran, hadith, and the practice of the Companions. By understanding these conditions, you will know exactly when an act of freeing a slave is legally effective in Hanafi law.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The act is emancipation (itq). The ruling is that it is valid (sahih) when the conditions are met; reward is a separate matter.
2
List the four conditions
From the matn: owner must be free (hurr), adult (baligh), sane (aaqil), and the slave must be in his ownership (fi milkihi).
These conditions apply to the owner, not the slave.
3
Trace the proof for each condition
For freedom/ownership: hadith 'la itq illa fima tamlik' (no emancipation except in what you own). For adulthood/sanity: hadith 'rufi'a al-qalam' (the pen is lifted from three) and the saying of 'Uthman. For virtue: Quran 90:12-14 and hadith on reward.
4
Understand the legal consequence
If any condition is missing, the emancipation is invalid (batil). For example, a minor's or slave's emancipation has no effect.
5
Confirm the Hanafi consensus
All Hanafi imams agree on these conditions; they are mufta bihi and based on clear textual evidence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كتاب العتاق

ضروری نوٹ عقاق کا معنی آزاد کرنا، آزاد کرنے کا ثبوت اور فضیلت اس آیت میں ہے۔ وما ادراك ما العقبة فك رقبة او اطعام في يوم ذي مسغبة ( آیت ۱۲ ۱۳ ۱۴، سورة البلد ۹۰ ) (۲) اور حدیث میں ہے قال ابو هريرة قال النبي له ايما رجل اعتق امرء مسلما استنقذ الله بكل عضو منه عضوا من النار (الف) ( بخاری شریف، باب فی العتق وفضله ص ۳۴۲ نمبر ۲۵۱۷) اس آیت اور حدیث سے معلوم ہوا کہ غلام باندی کو آزاد کرنا چاہئے اس سے ثواب ملتا ہے۔

[۲۱۹۰] (۱) آزادگی واقع ہوتی ہے آزاد، بالغ ، عاقل سے اس کی ملکیت میں۔ ر آدمی آزاد ہو، بالغ ہو اور عاقل ہو اور غلام باندی اس کی ملکیت میں ہو پھر اپنے غلام باندی کو آزاد کرے تو اس سے غلام یا باندی آزاد ہو جائے گا۔
آزادگی کی شرط اس لئے لگائی کہ غلام کے پاس تو کوئی چیز ہوتی ہی نہیں ہے۔ جو کچھ ہے وہ اس کے مولی کی ملکیت ہے۔ اس لئے مثلا تجارت کی اجازت دیئے ہوئے غلام کے پاس غلام ہو اور اس کو آزاد کرنا چاہے تو اس سے آزادگی واقع نہیں ہوگی (۲) حدیث میں اس کی تفریح ہے۔ عن عمر بن شعيب عن ابيه عن جده ان النبي قال لا طلاق الا فيما تملك ولا عتق الا فيما تملك ولا بيع الا فيما تملک (ب) (ابوداؤد شریف، باب فی الطلاق قبل النکاح ص ۳۰۵ نمبر ۲۱۹۰) اس حدیث سے معلوم ہوا کہ جس غلام کا مالک نہ ہو اس کو آزاد نہیں کر سکتا۔ اور غلام آدمی غلام باندی کا مالک نہیں ہوتا اس لئے وہ آزاد نہیں کر سکتا۔ اور بچے اور مجنون کی آزادگی اس لئے صحیح نہیں ہے کہ ان کو عقل نہیں ہوتی (۲) حدیث میں ہے۔ عن على عن النبى قال رفع القلم عن ثلاثة عن النائم حتى يستيقظ وعـن الـصبـى حـتـى يـحـتـلـم و عـن المجنون حتى يعقل (ج) ( ابوداؤد شریف، باب فی المجحون يسرق اور صیب حداص ۲۵۶ نمبر ۴۴۰۳ ) اس حدیث سے معلوم ہوا کہ بچے اور مجنون کی آزادگی کا اعتبار نہیں ہے (۳) بخاری میں قول صحابی ہے۔ وقال عثمان لیس لمجنون ولا سكران طلاق (و) ( بخاری شریف، باب الطلاق في الاغلاق والکر ص ۷۹۳ نمبر ۵۲۶۹) اس اثر سے بھی معلوم ہوا کہ بچے
اور مجنون کے طلاق اور عناق کا اعتبار نہیں ہے۔ اور ملکیت میں ہو اس کی دلیل ابو داؤد کی حدیث لا عتق الا فيما تملک گزر چکی ہے۔

حاشیہ : (الف) آپ نے فرمایا کوئی بھی آدمی مسلمان کو آزاد کرے تو اللہ اس کے ہر عضو کے بدلے آگ سے اس کے عضو کو آزاد کریں گے (ب ) آپ نے فرمایا نہیں طلاق واقع ہوگی مگر جس چیز کا مالک ہو، اور نہیں آزادگی ہے مگر جس چیز کا مالک ہو، اور نہیں بیچ ہے مگر جس چیز کا مالک ہو (ج) پ نے فرمایا تین آدمیوں سے قلم اٹھالیا گیا ہے۔ سونے والے سے جب تک کہ بیدار نہ ہو اور بچے سے یہاں تک کہ بالغ نہ ہو جائے اور مجنون سے یہاں تک کہ مجھدار نہ ہو جائے ( د ) حضرت عثمان نے فرمایا مجنون کے لئے اور مست کے لئے طلاق واقع نہیں ہے۔
Book of Emancipation. Necessary note: The meaning of itq is to free. The proof and virtue of freeing is in this verse: 'And what will make you know what the steep path is? (It is) the freeing of a slave, or feeding on a day of severe hunger' (Surah al-Balad, 90:12-14). And in hadith: Abu Huraira said: The Prophet said: 'Any man who frees a Muslim slave, Allah will free for every limb of his a limb from the Fire' (Bukhari, Bab al-Itq wa Fadlihi, p. 342, no. 2517). From this verse and hadith it is known that one should free a slave, and there is reward in it. [2190] (1) Emancipation occurs from a free, adult, sane person regarding his property. A man who is free, adult, and sane, and the slave is in his ownership, then if he frees his slave, the slave becomes free. The condition of freedom is set because a slave possesses nothing; whatever he has is the property of his master. So for example, if a slave who has been given permission to trade owns a slave and wants to free him, emancipation does not occur. (2) There is clarification in hadith: 'Umar ibn Shu'ayb from his father from his grandfather that the Prophet said: 'No divorce except concerning what you own, and no emancipation except concerning what you own, and no sale except concerning what you own' (Abu Dawud, Bab al-Talaq qabl al-Nikah, p. 305, no. 2190). From this hadith it is known that one cannot free a slave one does not own. And a slave man does not own a slave, so he cannot free. And the emancipation of a child and an insane person is not valid because they lack intellect. (2) In hadith: 'Ali from the Prophet said: 'The pen is lifted from three: from the sleeper until he wakes, from the child until he reaches puberty, and from the insane until he recovers reason' (Abu Dawud, Bab al-Majnun Yassiruq wa... p. 256, no. 4403). From this hadith it is known that the emancipation of a child and an insane person is not considered. (3) In Bukhari, the saying of a Companion: 'Uthman said: 'There is no divorce for an insane person nor for an intoxicated person' (Bukhari, Bab al-Talaq fi al-Ighlaq wa al-Kur, p. 793, no. 5269). From this athar also it is known that the divorce and emancipation of a child and an insane person are not considered. And the proof for ownership is the hadith of Abu Dawud: 'No emancipation except concerning what you own' which has passed. Footnote: (a) He said: Any man who frees a Muslim, Allah will free from the Fire for each limb his limb. (b) He said: No divorce occurs except concerning what you own, and no emancipation except concerning what you own, and no sale except concerning what you own. (c) He said: The pen has been lifted from three: from the sleeper until he wakes, from the child until he reaches puberty, and from the insane until he becomes sane. (d) 'Uthman said: No divorce occurs for an insane person nor for an intoxicated person.
Source pages p. 240

Commentary
This is the opening of the Book of Emancipation (Kitab al-'Itaq). The author begins by establishing the virtue of freeing slaves through Quranic verse (90:12-14) and the famous hadith from Abu Huraira (Bukhari) about freeing a Muslim slave being a means of salvation. Then the first ruling: emancipation is valid only from a free, adult, sane owner over his own slave. The commentary explains each condition: the owner must be free because a slave himself cannot own property; thus a slave cannot free another slave. The owner must be adult and sane because children and the insane are not legally responsible (pen lifted). Proofs from hadith: no emancipation except over what you own (Abu Dawud), and the hadith of the lifted pen. Also from the practice of 'Uthman regarding divorce. The ruling is clear: all four conditions must be met for a valid emancipation.

Key Terms
العتق (al-‘itq): Emancipation, freeing a slave from bondage.
الحر (al-hurr): Free person, not a slave.
البالغ العاقل (al-baaligh al-‘aaqil): Adult (reached puberty) and sane.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Key Takeaway
Emancipation in Hanafi law is valid only when performed by a free, adult, sane owner over his own slave.

Explicit Phrases of Emancipation and the Role of Intention
Listing explicit emancipation terms and proving their independence from intention

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between explicit (sareeh) and allusive (kinayah) words of emancipation, state the Hanafi ruling for each, and identify the Quranic and Hadith proofs that establish sareeh words as independent of intention.

Core Idea
Explicit words of emancipation free the slave even without intention; allusive words require intention.

The source text lists the explicit Arabic phrases that effect emancipation and explains why intention is irrelevant for them. It also covers cases where the master mentions a body part and the rule for allusive statements. Understanding this distinction is crucial for applying Hanafi rulings on emancipation correctly.

Guided Path
1
Identify the explicit words and their proofs
List the six explicit phrases from the matn (anta hurr, mu'taq, atiq, muharrar, harrartuka, a'taqtuka). Note the Quranic proof (Surah an-Nisa 4:92) using 'tahreer' and the Hadith proof (Bukhari) using 'a'taqa'.
These words are considered sareeh because they are directly derived from revelation.
2
Understand the 'body part' cases
The passage extends the ruling to body parts like head, neck, body, and private part, reasoning that they imply the whole slave, analogous to the Quranic use of 'raqabah' to mean the entire slave.
The principle: if the part mentioned is conventionally used to refer to the whole person, emancipation applies to the whole slave.
3
Distinguish explicit from allusive
The final section introduces kinayah words (e.g., 'I have no ownership over you') and states that they require explicit intention (niyyah) to effect emancipation. Compare with sareeh where intention is irrelevant.
This mirrors the rule in divorce: sareeh words are independent of intention, kinayah require it.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
[۲۱۹۱](۲) فاذا قال لعبده او امته انت حر او معتق او عتیق او محرر او حررتک او اعتقتك فقد عتق نوي المولى العتق او لم ينو (۲۱۹۲ (۳) و كذلک اذا قال رأسک حر او رقبتک او بدنک او قال لامته فرجک حر [ ۲۱۹۳](۴) وان قال لا ملک لی علیک و نوى بذلك الحرية عتق

(۲۱۹۱) (۲) پس اگر اپنے غلام اور باندی سے کہا تو آزاد ہے یا آزاد کیا ہوا ہے یا میں نے تجھ کو آزاد کیا تو آزاد ہو جائے گا۔ آقا نے آزادگی کی نیت کی ہو یا نہ کی ہو۔
عربی زبان میں آزاد کرنے کے یہ سب جملے ہیں کہ ان سب جملوں کو استعمال کرنے سے آزادگی واقع ہو جائے گی۔ اور چونکہ یہ الفاظ صریح ہیں اس لئے نیت کرے یا نہ کرے ہر حال میں آزادگی واقع ہو جائے گی ۔ حر کا لفظ صریح ہے اس کی دلیل یہ آیت ہے ومن قتل مؤمنا خطاء فتحرير رقبة مؤمنة (الف) ( آیت ۹۲ سورۃ النساء۴ ) اس آیت میں آزادگی کے لئے تحریر کا لفظ استعمال ہوا ہے جو صریح ہے۔ اور عشق کے صریح ہونے کے لئے یہ حدیث ہے۔ قال لى ابو هرية قال النبى ايما رجل اعتق امرء مسلما استنقذ الله بكل عضو منه عضوا من النار (ب) ( بخاری شریف، باب فی العشق وفضلہ ص ۳۴۲ نمبر ۲۵۱۷) اس حدیث میں محقق کا لفظ صریح ہے۔ اور انہیں دونوں لفظوں سے باقی جملے بنے ہیں اس لئے وہ جملے بھی صریح ہوئے۔ اس لئے ان جملوں سے بغیر نیت کئے ہوئے بھی طلاق واقع ہو جائیگی۔
آزاد، محق بحق سے اسم مفعول ہے آزاد کیا ہوا ہے، تحقیق فعیل کے وزن پر اسم مفعول ہے آزاد کیا ہوا محرر : آزاد کیا ہوا یہ بھی اسم مفعول ہے، حررت: میں نے آزاد کیا، اعتقت : میں نے تجھے آزاد کیا۔

(۲۱۹۲) (۳) ایسے ہی اگر کہا تیرا سر آزاد یا تیری گردن آزاد یا تیرا بدن آزاد یا اپنی باندی سے کہا تیری شرمگاہ آزاد تو آزاد ہو جائے گا۔ یہ مسائل اس اصول پر ہیں کہ ایسے عضو کے بارے میں کہا کہ وہ آزاد ہے جس سے پورا جسم مراد لیتے ہیں تو اس سے پورا جسم مراد لیکر غلام یا باندی آزاد ہو جائیں گے۔ مثلا کہا کہ تیرا سر آزاد ہے تو اس سے پورا غلام آزاد ہو جائے گا۔ کیونکہ سر بول کر پورا انسان مراد لیتے ہیں۔ آیت میں ہے۔ ومن قتل مـومـنـا خطاء فتحرير رقبة مؤمنة (ج) (آیت ۹۲ سورة النساء ۴ ) اس آیت میں رقبہ بول کر پورا انسان مراد لیا ہے۔ اس لئے گردن بول کر پورا انسان مراد لیتے ہیں۔ باقی تفصیل کتاب الطلاق مسئلہ نمبر اس میں دیکھ لیں۔

(۲۱۹۳) (۴) اور اگر کہا کہ میری آپ پر ملکیت نہیں ہے اور اس سے آزادگی کی نیت کی تو آزاد ہو جائے گا۔ اور اگر نیت نہیں کی تو آزاد نہیں ہوگا اور یہی حال آزادگی کے تمام کنائی الفاظ کا ہے۔ آزاد کرنے کے لئے الفاظ کنایہ استعمال کئے تو اگر اس سے آزاد کرنے کی نیت ہو تو آزاد ہو جائے گا۔ اور اگر آزاد کرنے کی نیت نہ ہو تو آزاد نہیں ہوگا۔

حاشیہ : (الف) اور اگر کسی نے مومن کو غلطی سے قتل کیا تو اس کے بدلے مومن غلام کو آزاد کیا جائے (ب) آپ نے فرمایا کوئی آدمی مسلمان کو آزاد کیا تو ہر عضو کے بدلے اللہ آگ سے آزاد کرے گا (ج) کسی نے مومن آدمی کو غلطی سے قتل کیا تو اس کے کفارے میں مومن غلام کو آزاد کرنا ہے۔
[2191] (2) So if he says to his male slave or female slave: 'You are free' or 'freed' or 'freeman' or 'emancipated' or 'I freed you' or 'I have freed you', then he becomes free, whether the master intended emancipation or not. [2192] (3) Similarly, if he says: 'Your head is free' or 'your neck' or 'your body' or he says to his female slave: 'Your private part is free', then he becomes free. [2193] (4) And if he says: 'I have no ownership over you' and intends by that freedom, he becomes free.

(2191) So if he says to his male or female slave: 'You are free' or 'freed' or 'I freed you', then he becomes free, whether the master intended emancipation or not. All these are phrases in the Arabic language for freeing; using any of them causes emancipation. Since these words are explicit, emancipation occurs whether he intends or not. The word 'free' (hurr) is explicit; its proof is the verse: 'And whoever kills a believer by mistake, then the freeing of a believing slave' (Surah an-Nisa, 4:92). In this verse the word 'tahreer' is used for emancipation, which is explicit. And for the explicitness of 'itq, there is this hadith: Abu Huraira said to me: The Prophet said: 'Any man who frees (a'taqa) a Muslim slave, Allah will free for every limb of his a limb from the Fire' (Bukhari). In this hadith the word a'taqa is explicit. And from these two words, the rest of the phrases are derived, so they are also explicit. Therefore, from these phrases, emancipation occurs even without intention. 'Anta hurr': you are free; 'mu'taq': passive participle, freed; 'atiq': on the pattern of fa'il, passive meaning; 'muharrar': freed, also passive; 'harrartuka': I freed you; 'a'taqtuka': I have freed you.

(2192) Similarly, if he says: 'Your head is free' or 'your neck' or 'your body' or to his female slave: 'Your private part is free', then he becomes free. These cases are based on the principle that if he says a part that implies the whole body, then the whole slave is intended and becomes free. For example, he said 'your head is free', then the entire slave becomes free, because by 'head' the whole person is meant. In the verse: 'And whoever kills a believer by mistake, then the freeing of a believing neck (raqabah)' (Surah an-Nisa, 4:92), by 'neck' the whole person is meant. So by 'neck' the whole person is intended. For further detail, see the Book of Divorce, issue number...

(2193) And if he says: 'I have no ownership over you' and intends freedom by it, he becomes free. If he does not intend, he does not become free. This is the rule for all kinayah (allusive) words of emancipation. If kinayah words are used for freeing, then if he intends freedom, it occurs; if he does not, it does not.

Footnotes: (a) And if someone kills a believer by mistake, then as expiation he must free a believing slave. (b) He said: Any man who frees a Muslim, Allah will free from the Fire for each limb his limb. (c) Someone who kills a believer by mistake must free a believing slave as expiation.
Source pages p. 240

Commentary
This passage covers three rulings on the wording of emancipation. First (2191): The explicit (sareeh) words of emancipation are listed: 'anta hurr', 'mu'taq', 'atiq', 'muharrar', 'harrartuka', 'a'taqtuka'. These are considered explicit because they are derived from the Quranic word 'tahreer' and the hadith word 'itq'. When any of these is used, the slave becomes free regardless of the master's intention. Second (2192): If the master mentions a body part that is synonymous with the whole person (like head, neck, body, or private part in the case of a female slave), the entire slave is emancipated. The proof is the Quranic use of 'raqabah' (neck) to mean a whole slave. Third (2193): Kinayah (allusive) words, like 'I have no ownership over you', require intention. If the master intends emancipation, the slave is free; otherwise not. This is analogous to kinayah in divorce. The commentary emphasizes the distinction between explicit and allusive phrasing. All rulings are from the Hanafi school.

Key Terms
صريح (sareeh): Explicit wording that clearly indicates emancipation without need for intention.
كناية (kinayah): Allusive wording that requires intention to effect emancipation.
رقبة (raqabah): Literally 'neck', used in Quran to mean a whole slave.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the ruling if a master says to his slave 'Your head is free'?
A) Only the head is freed, not the rest of the body.
B) The entire slave is freed because 'head' implies the whole person.
C) The slave is not freed unless the master also mentions other parts.
D) It depends on the master's intention.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, explicit words of emancipation are effective regardless of intention, while allusive words require intention.

Explicit Phrases of Emancipation (Sarih) in Hanafi Fiqh
States that explicit verbal phrases effect emancipation without intention

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify sarih phrases of emancipation in Hanafi fiqh and explain why they require no intention, citing the Quranic and hadith evidence.

Core Idea
Sarih phrases of emancipation effect freeing immediately, with or without intention.

The Hanafi school distinguishes between sarih (explicit) and kinayah (implicit) expressions in matters of emancipation and divorce. This lesson examines the ruling that certain clear verbal phrases—such as 'you are free' or 'I emancipate you'—are legally binding regardless of the speaker's inner intent. We will explore the textual proof from the Quran and Sunnah that establishes this rule.

Guided Path
1
Identify the ruling
The passage states that sarih phrases of emancipation effect the freeing of the slave immediately, whether the master intended emancipation or not.
2
List the sarih phrases
The text enumerates six sarih phrases: 'anti hurr', 'mu'taq', 'atiq', 'muhrar', 'harrartu', and 'a'taqtu'. These are all derived from two root words: 'tahrir' and 'itq'.
The grammatical forms include passive participles and past tense verbs.
3
Understand the basis for sarih
The root word 'tahrir' appears in Quran 4:92 (freeing a slave as expiation), and 'itq' appears in the hadith of Abu Huraira. Because these words are used in authoritative texts for emancipation, any derived form is considered explicit.
The Quranic verse provides the dalil for 'hurr', and the hadith provides the dalil for 'itq'.
4
Apply the Hanafi principle
In Hanafi usul, sarih expressions operate purely on linguistic clarity; therefore, intention (niyyah) is not required. This contrasts with kinayah, where intention is necessary.
The principle of 'al-umur bi-maqasidiha' applies to kinayah, not sarih.
5
Note the cross-madhhab difference
Imam al-Shafi'i (in one view) requires intention even for sarih phrases. The Hanafi school rejects this, relying on the linguistic certainty of the expressions.
The practical difference: if a master says 'you are free' without intending it, the Hanafi school holds the slave is free, while the Shafi'i view may not.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
امته انت حر او معتق او عتیق او محرر او حررتک او اعتقتك فقد عتق نوي المولى العتق او لم ينو (۲۱۹۲ (۳) عربی زبان میں آزاد کرنے کے یہ سب جملے ہیں کہ ان سب جملوں کو استعمال کرنے سے آزادگی واقع ہو جائے گی۔ اور چونکہ یہ الفاظ صریح ہیں اس لئے نیت کرے یا نہ کرے ہر حال میں آزادگی واقع ہو جائے گی ۔ حر کا لفظ صریح ہے اس کی دلیل یہ آیت ہے ومن قتل مؤمنا خطاء فتحرير رقبة مؤمنة ( آیت ۹۲ سورۃ النساء۴ ) اس آیت میں آزادگی کے لئے تحریر کا لفظ استعمال ہوا ہے جو صریح ہے۔ اور عتق کے صریح ہونے کے لئے یہ حدیث ہے۔ قال لى ابو هرية قال النبى ايما رجل اعتق امرء مسلما استنقذ الله بكل عضو منه عضوا من النار ( بخاری شریف، باب فی العتق وفضلہ ص ۳۴۲ نمبر ۲۵۱۷) اس حدیث میں معتق کا لفظ صریح ہے۔ اور انہیں دونوں لفظوں سے باقی جملے بنے ہیں اس لئے وہ جملے بھی صریح ہوئے۔ اس لئے ان جملوں سے بغیر نیت کئے ہوئے بھی عتق واقع ہو جائیگی۔ آزاد، محق بحق سے اسم مفعول ہے آزاد کیا ہوا ہے، تحقیق فعیل کے وزن پر اسم مفعول ہے آزاد کیا ہوا محرر : آزاد کیا ہوا یہ بھی اسم مفعول ہے، حررت: میں نے آزاد کیا، اعتقت : میں نے تجھے آزاد کیا۔
His slave girl: you are free, or you are emancipated, or you are freed, or you are liberated, or I have freed you, or I have emancipated you—then she is free, whether the master intended emancipation or not. In the Arabic language, these are all sentences for freeing. Using any of these sentences, emancipation occurs. Because these words are explicit, so whether he intends or not, emancipation occurs in all cases. The word 'hurr' is explicit; its proof is the verse: 'And whoever kills a believer by mistake then the freeing of a believing slave' (Surah Nisa 4:92). In this verse, the word 'tahrir' is used for emancipation, which is explicit. And for the explicitness of 'itq', there is this hadith: Abu Huraira said: The Prophet said: 'Any man who emancipates a Muslim, Allah will save from the fire every limb of his in exchange for every limb of the emancipated person' (Sahih Bukhari, Book of Emancipation, p.342 no.2517). In this hadith, the word 'mu'tiq' is explicit. And from these two words, the remaining sentences are derived, so those sentences are also explicit. Therefore, from these sentences, emancipation occurs even without intention. 'Hurr' is a passive participle from 'tahrir', meaning freed; 'mutaq' is a passive participle on the pattern of fa'il, meaning freed; 'muhrar' means freed; 'harrartu' means I freed; 'a'taqtu' means I freed you.
Source pages p. 241

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with proof. It establishes that explicit (sarih) phrases of emancipation, such as 'anti hurr' (you are free) or 'a'taqtuka' (I emancipated you), effect the freeing of the slave immediately, regardless of whether the master intended emancipation or not. The commentary explains that these phrases are considered sarih because they derive from two root words used in the Quran and Sunnah: 'tahrir' (freeing) from the Quranic verse on kaffarah for accidental killing, and 'itq' (emancipation) from the hadith of Abu Huraira. Since the Quran and the Prophet used these words in the context of emancipation, they are unambiguous. Therefore, any verbal form derived from them carries the same explicitness, and no separate intention (niyyah) is needed. The commentary also provides the morphological breakdown of each term: hurr (passive participle), mu'taq (passive participle), muhrar (passive participle), harrartu (first person past tense), a'taqtu (first person past tense). The ruling is that these phrases are effective as sarih expressions.

Key Terms
صریح (sarih): Clear and unambiguous verbal expression that effects emancipation without need for intention.
عتق (itq): Emancipation of a slave; freeing from bondage.
حر (hurr): Free person; also used as a predicate in emancipation: 'you are free'.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) 'I have no ownership over you' (with intention)
B) 'Your head is free'
C) 'You are free' (anti hurr)
D) 'I have no authority over you' (with intention)

Key Takeaway
Sarih phrases of emancipation derived from the Quranic word 'tahrir' and the hadith word 'itq' are legally effective without intention in the Hanafi school.

Body Part Phrases and Full Emancipation
Ruling with proof: body parts emancipate wholly

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling that mentioning a body part implying the whole person effects full emancipation, and identify the proof from Quran 4:92.

Core Idea
Saying 'your head is free' frees the entire slave because customary usage equates the part with the whole.

In classical Hanafi law, a master's words can set a slave free. While direct phrases like 'you are free' are clear, passages like this one show that even mentioning a body part—when that part conventionally stands for the whole person—creates a complete emancipation. This lesson unpacks the principle and its Quranic foundation.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The act is the master uttering a phrase referring to a body part (e.g., 'your head is free'). The ruling is that the entire slave becomes free immediately.
This is a one-step ruling: the utterance is the cause of emancipation.
2
Examine the underlying principle
The principle is that if a body part is customarily used to refer to the whole person, then mentioning that part in the context of emancipation means the whole slave is freed.
Common parts: head (ra's), neck (raqabah), body (badan), private part (farj).
3
Trace the Quranic proof
The author cites Quran 4:92, where Allah says 'freeing a believing neck' (raqabah) to mean freeing a whole slave. This shows that the word 'neck' (raqabah) stands for the entire person in Islamic legal usage.
The dalil is dalil naqli: the Quran itself uses a body part to mean the whole.
4
Apply the principle to the examples
Apply the same reasoning: if 'raqabah' (neck) means the whole slave, then 'head', 'body', and 'private part' also function similarly. Each triggers full emancipation.
The extension relies on 'urf (customary usage), not on a direct textual mention of each part.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
و کذلک اذا قال رأسک حر او رقبتک او بدنک او قال لامته فرجک حر [ ۲۱۹۳](۴) ایسے ہی اگر کہا تیرا سر آزاد یا تیری گردن آزاد یا تیرا بدن آزاد یا اپنی باندی سے کہا تیری شرمگاہ آزاد تو آزاد ہو جائے گا۔ یہ مسائل اس اصول پر ہیں کہ ایسے عضو کے بارے میں کہا کہ وہ آزاد ہے جس سے پورا جسم مراد لیتے ہیں تو اس سے پورا جسم مراد لیکر غلام یا باندی آزاد ہو جائیں گے۔ مثلا کہا کہ تیرا سر آزاد ہے تو اس سے پورا غلام آزاد ہو جائے گا۔ کیونکہ سر بول کر پورا انسان مراد لیتے ہیں۔ آیت میں ہے۔ ومن قتل مـومـنـا خطاء فتحرير رقبة مؤمنة (ج) (آیت ۹۲ سورة النساء ۴ ) اس آیت میں رقبہ بول کر پورا انسان مراد لیا ہے۔ اس لئے گردن بول کر پورا انسان مراد لیتے ہیں۔ باقی تفصیل کتاب الطلاق مسئلہ نمبر اس میں دیکھ لیں۔ حاشیہ : (ج) کسی نے مومن آدمی کو غلطی سے قتل کیا تو اس کے کفارے میں مومن غلام کو آزاد کرنا ہے۔
And similarly, if he says: your head is free, or your neck, or your body, or if he says to his slave girl: your private part is free, then she becomes free. These issues are based on the principle that if one says about a limb that is used to refer to the whole body, then it means the whole body, so the male or female slave becomes free. For example, if he says: your head is free, then the entire slave becomes free because by saying 'head' one means the whole person. In the verse: 'And whoever kills a believer by mistake then the freeing of a believing neck' (Surah Nisa 4:92), by saying 'neck' the whole person is meant. Therefore, by saying 'neck' one means the whole person. For the remaining details, see the book of divorce, the relevant issue number. Footnote (j): If someone kills a believing person by mistake, then as its expiation, a believing slave must be freed.
Source pages p. 241

Commentary
This passage presents a ruling with proof. It extends the principle of explicit emancipation to phrases that mention a specific body part that is conventionally used to refer to the entire human being. For example, saying 'your head is free' or 'your neck is free' or 'your body is free' or even 'your private part is free' to a slave girl results in the complete emancipation of the slave. The rationale is that these body parts—head, neck, body, private part—are used metaphorically or metonymically in Arabic to represent the whole person. The commentary provides a Quranic proof: in verse 4:92, Allah uses the word 'raqabah' (neck) to mean a whole slave ('a believing slave'), demonstrating that a part can stand for the whole. Therefore, if a master says 'your neck is free,' it is equivalent to saying 'you are free.' The commentary also directs the reader to the book of divorce for further details, implying a similar principle applies there. The footnote provides the translation of the Quranic verse.

Key Terms
رقبة (raqabah): Literally 'neck', but used in Islamic law to mean a whole slave (a human being in bondage).
كنایه (kinayah): Indirect or ambiguous expression that requires intention for emancipation. Contrast with sarih.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi law, mentioning a body part that customarily stands for the whole person (head, neck, body, private part) effects full emancipation of the slave, based on Quranic usage and 'urf.

Kinayah in Emancipation: Intention is Key
States that kinayah phrases like 'I have no ownership over you' require intention

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why kinayah phrases in emancipation require intention (niyyah) and apply the hadith of Rukanah to distinguish between sarih and kinayah expressions.

Core Idea
Kinayah phrases for emancipation need intention; without it, no freedom.

In Hanafi fiqh, a master can free a slave by uttering certain words. Some words are clear (sarih) and work automatically; others are ambiguous (kinayah) and require a specific intention. This lesson explains the ruling on kinayah phrases like 'I have no ownership over you' and the proof from the Prophet's questioning of Rukanah about his intention.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and ruling
The act is the master saying a kinayah phrase to a slave. The ruling: emancipation occurs only if the master intended it at the time of utterance.
Kinayah has multiple meanings; intention selects the emancipation meaning.
2
Locate the dalil
The proof is the hadith of Rukanah, where the Prophet asked about his intention when he used the kinayah word 'al-battah' for divorce.
The hadith is ahad (zanni al-thubut) but sufficient for establishing the rule.
3
Trace the wajh al-dalalah
The Prophet's inquiry about intention shows that for kinayah, intention is determinative. The same principle applies by qiyas to emancipation.
The ruling is derived from the explicit question 'What did you intend?'
4
Note the ikhtilaf
Some Shafi'is hold that even sarih phrases require intention. The Hanafi school distinguishes using linguistic clarity.
The disagreement is on the scope of the niyyah requirement.
5
Evaluate the tarjih
The Hanafi position is mufta bihi, supported by the practice of the Companions and the clear linguistic difference between sarih and kinayah.
The hadith of Rukanah is specifically about kinayah, not sarih.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وان قال لا ملک لی علیک و نوى بذلك الحرية عتق نیت کی ہو یا نہ کی ہو۔ کنایہ کے الفاظ کے دو معنی ہوتے ہیں۔ ایک معنی سے آزادگی ہوگی اور دوسرے معنی لینے سے آزادگی نہیں ہوگی اس لئے آزاد کرنے کے لئے نیت کرنا ہو گا۔ مثلا میری تم پر ملکیت نہیں ہے کا ایک معنی یہ ہے کہ تم کو بیچ دیا اس لئے میری تم پر ملکیت نہیں ہے۔ اس سے آزادگی نہیں ہوگی۔ اور دوسرا معنی ہے کہ میں نے تم کو آزاد کر دیا اس لئے میری تم پر ملکیت نہیں ہے۔ اس لئے اس معنی لینے سے آزاد ہو جائے گا۔ لیکن اس معنی لینے کے لئے نیت کرنی ہوگی۔ حضرت رکانہ نے بیوی کو طلاق دینے کے لئے لفظ کنایہ استعمال کیا تھا تو حضور نے اس کی نیت پوچھی ۔ پھر انہوں نے کہا کہ ایک طلاق کی نیت کی ہے اس لئے ایک طلاق واقع کی۔ حدیث یہ ہے۔ عن عبد الله بن على بن يزيد ركانة عن ابيه عن جده انه طلق امرأته البتة فاتى رسول الله الله الله فقال ما اردت؟ قال واحدة قال الله؟ قال الله! قال هو على ما اردت (ابوداؤد شریف، باب فی البیتی ص ۳۰۷ نمبر ۲۲۰۸، ترمذی شریف ، باب ما جاء فی الرجل يطلق امرأته البيتة ص ۲۲۲ نمبر ۱۱۷۷) اس حدیث میں البتہ کا لفظ کنایہ استعمال کیا ہے اس لئے آپ نے رکانہ سے نیت پوچھی۔ اصول تمام کنائی الفاظ میں یہی اصول ہے کہ نیت کرے گا تو آزادگی واقع ہوگی ورنہ نہیں۔ (۲۱۹۴ (۵) وان قال لا سلطان لي عليه ونوى به العتق لم يعتق[۲۱۹۵]
And if he says: I have no ownership over you, and he intends thereby freedom, then she becomes free, whether his intention was or not. Kinayah words have two meanings. With one meaning, emancipation occurs; with the other, it does not. Therefore, one must have the intention to free. For example, 'I have no ownership over you' can mean: I have sold you, so I have no ownership over you—this does not bring emancipation. The other meaning is: I have freed you, so I have no ownership over you. So by taking this meaning, she becomes free, but one must intend this meaning. Sayyiduna Rukanah used a kinayah word for divorcing his wife, so the Prophet asked him his intention. He said: I intended one divorce. The Prophet said: By Allah? He said: By Allah! He said: It is as you intended. (Abu Dawud, Kitab al-Bayt?, p.307 no.2208; Tirmidhi, Bab ma ja'a fi al-rajul yutalliqu imra'atahu al-battah, p.222 no.1177). In this hadith, the word 'al-battah' is used as kinayah, so the Prophet asked Rukanah his intention. The principle for all kinayah words is that if he intends emancipation, it occurs; otherwise not. (2194)(5) And if he says: I have no authority over him, and intends thereby emancipation, he does not become free.
Source pages p. 241

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with proof concerning kinayah (ambiguous) phrases of emancipation. It first states that if the master says 'I have no ownership over you' (la mulka li 'alayki) and intends emancipation, then the slave becomes free. The commentary explains that kinayah words have multiple possible meanings; only if the master intends the meaning of emancipation does it take effect. For instance, the sentence 'I have no ownership over you' could mean he has sold the slave, or that he has freed him. Without intention, the phrase remains ambiguous. The proof is a hadith about divorce: when Rukanah said 'al-battah' (a kinayah for divorce), the Prophet asked him what he intended. When Rukanah said 'one divorce', the Prophet confirmed it. This hadith establishes that for kinayah expressions, intention is necessary. The same principle applies to emancipation: all kinayah phrases require intention. The passage ends with a brief mention of the next ruling (about 'la sultana li 'alayh'), which is a separate case.

Key Terms
کنایه (kinayah): An ambiguous or indirect expression that can imply emancipation or something else; requires intention.
نیت (niyyah): Intent in the heart; for kinayah phrases, the specific intention to emancipate is necessary.
صریح (sarih): Clear, unambiguous expression that directly conveys the intended meaning without need for intention.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If a master says to his slave 'I have no ownership over you' without intending emancipation, what is the ruling?
A) The slave becomes free regardless
B) The slave does not become free
C) The slave becomes free only if the master is just
D) The phrase is sarih, so emancipation occurs

Key Takeaway
Kinayah phrases require intention for emancipation; sarih phrases do not.

Phrase 'No Authority Over You' Does Not Emancipate
Ruling on a specific kinayah phrase that fails to emancipate even with intention.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on the kinayah phrase 'la sultana li 'alayh' and explain why it does not effect emancipation even when accompanied by intention.

Core Idea
The phrase 'I have no authority over you' does not free a slave, even if intended, because it is ambiguous when referring to a mukatib.

In Hanafi fiqh, certain kinayah phrases can effect emancipation if accompanied by intention. However, the phrase 'la sultana li 'alayh' is an exception: it never works, even with clear intent. Why? Because it could be interpreted as referring to a mukatib slave, over whom the master indeed has limited authority. This lesson unpacks that ruling and its rationale.

Guided Path
1
Identify the phrase and its literal meaning
Read the Arabic phrase 'لا سلطان لي عليه' (la sultana li 'alayh), which translates to 'I have no authority over him.' Recognize this as a kinayah (allusive) expression, not a صريح (explicit) statement like 'أنت حر' (you are free).
2
Note the presence of intention (niyyah)
The text explicitly says 'ونوى به العتق' (and intends thereby emancipation). In Hanafi law, intention can turn many kinayah phrases into effective emancipations, but not all.
3
Understand why intention does not help here
The reason (found in longer commentaries) is that a mukatib (a slave under contract to buy freedom) also lacks full authority from his master. Since the phrase could truthfully refer to a mukatib, it is ambiguous and cannot be considered a declaration of freedom.
This is an exception to the general rule that kinayah + niyyah = emancipation.
4
Contrast with effective kinayah phrases
Compare with phrases like 'أنت حر' (you are free) which are صريح and need no niyyah, or 'لا ملك لي عليك' (I have no ownership over you) which with niyyah does emancipate. The difference is that 'no authority' is shared by the mukatib scenario.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وان قال لا سلطان لي عليه ونوى به العتق لم يعتق[۲۱۹۵] (۲) واذا قال هذا ابنى وثبت على ذلك او قال هذا مولى او يا مولى عتق [۲۱۹۶]
And if he says: I have no authority over him, and intends thereby emancipation, he does not become free. (2195)(2) And if he says: This is my son and he is established on that, or he says: This is my master, or O my master, he becomes free.
Source pages p. 242

Commentary
This passage presents a ruling without a separate proof in this excerpt (though a proof may be found in the broader text). It states that the phrase 'I have no authority over him' (la sultana li 'alayh) does not effect emancipation even if the master intended freeing. The reason, as explained in the surrounding commentary (not fully captured here), is that a mukatib slave (who is partially free) also lacks authority over him, so the phrase is ambiguous. Hence, unlike other kinayah phrases, even intention does not make it effective. This is an exception to the general rule for kinayah. The passage then transitions to another ruling: saying 'this is my son' or 'this is my master' effects emancipation, which is the next lesson.

Key Terms
سلطان (sultan): Authority or power; here it means legal control over a slave.
مكاتب (mukatib): A slave who has contracted with his master to purchase his freedom by paying installments.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) The slave becomes free because intention is present
B) The slave does not become free
C) The slave becomes free only if the master repeats the phrase
D) The master must also say 'I free you' for it to work

Key Takeaway
The phrase 'I have no authority over you' is not a valid expression of emancipation, even with intention, because it can truthfully apply to a mukatib slave.

Acknowledging a Slave as Son or Master Frees Him
States that claiming a slave as son or master effects emancipation through lineage or mahram

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling when a master acknowledges a slave as his son or master, identify the conditions, and explain the proof from hadith and qiyas.

Core Idea
A master's serious claim of paternity to a slave of unknown lineage establishes a mahram relationship, freeing the slave.

This passage addresses a scenario where a master verbally acknowledges a slave as his son or master. The Hanafi school rules that this acknowledgment, when made seriously and under certain conditions, results in the slave's emancipation. The ruling is derived from a prophetic hadith and extended via qiyas (analogy). We will explore the conditions, the proof, and the reasoning behind this unique rule.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Ruling and Conditions
First, state that the slave becomes free. Then list the conditions: the slave's lineage must be unknown, the master must be serious (not joking), and the slave's age must be plausible for the claimed relationship (e.g., young enough to be a son).
The text explicitly mentions these conditions.
2
Understand the Role of Lineage
The key is that the acknowledgment establishes lineage. Since the slave's lineage was unknown, the master's statement makes the slave his son legally, creating a mahram relationship.
Mahram: a relative one cannot marry, like a son.
3
Examine the Hadith Proof
The hadith of Samurah ibn Jundub states: 'Whoever owns a close relative who is a mahram, he is free.' This is the textual basis. The hadith is ahad (singular) but accepted by Hanafi scholars.
The hadith is cited from Abu Dawud and Tirmidhi.
4
Apply Qiyas (Analogy)
The original case (asl) is ownership of a blood mahram relative, which frees them. The new case (far') is a master acknowledging a slave as his son (creating a legal mahram relationship). The effective cause (illa) is the existence of a mahram relationship between owner and owned.
Qiyas is used because the acknowledgment creates a legal lineage.
5
Note the Mufta Bihi Position and Cross-Madhhab Difference
The Hanafi position is that the slave is freed if conditions are met. Some scholars require the lineage to be unknown for the acknowledgment to take effect, which the Hanafis also hold. Other schools may have stricter views on acknowledging paternity.
This is the relied-upon (mufta bihi) position in the Hanafi school.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
واذا قال هذا ابنى وثبت على ذلك او قال هذا مولى او يا مولى عتق [۲۱۹۶] آقا سے غلام کی عمر اتنی کم ہے کہ اس جیسا غلام آقا کا بیٹا بن سکتا ہے اور غلام کا نسب بھی مشہور نہیں ہے ایسے غلام سے آقا کہے کہ یہ میرا بیٹا ہے اور یہ بات مذاق میں نہیں کہہ رہا ہے بلکہ حقیقت میں کہہ رہا ہے تو اس سے غلام آزاد ہو جائے گا۔ اس لئے کہ اس صورت میں غلام کا نسب آقا سے ثابت ہو جائے گا۔ کیونکہ غلام کا نسب مشہور نہیں ہے اور جب آقا سے نسب ثابت ہو گیا اور اس کا بیٹا ہو گیا تو قاعدہ من ملک ذی رحم محرم منه عتق کی وجہ سے غلام بیٹا بنے کی وجہ سے آزاد ہو جائے گا۔ حدیث میں ہے۔ عن سمرة بن جندب فيما يحسب . ، حماد قال قال رسول الله الا الله من ملك ذارحم محرم فهو حر (ابو داؤد شریف , باب فیمن ملک زارحم محرم ص ۱۹۴ نمبر ۱۳۹۴۹، ترمذی شریف، باب ماجاء فیمن ملک زار تم محرم ص ۲۵۳ نمبر ۱۳۶۵) اس حدیث سے
And if he says: This is my son and he is established on that, or he says: This is my master, or O my master, he becomes free. The slave's age is so young that such a slave could be the master's son, and the slave's lineage is not known. If the master says to such a slave 'This is my son' and he is not joking but speaking truthfully, then the slave becomes free. Because in this case, the slave's lineage becomes established from the master. Since the slave's lineage is unknown, and when it becomes established from the master, he becomes his son, then by the rule 'Whoever owns a close relative who is a mahram, he becomes free,' the slave becomes free due to becoming a son. In the hadith: Samurah ibn Jundub reported (as Hammad thought) that the Messenger of Allah said: 'Indeed, whoever owns a close relative who is a mahram, he is free.' (Abu Dawud, Bab fi man malaka dha rahim muhram, p.194 no.13949; Tirmidhi, Bab ma ja'a fi man malaka dha rahim muhram, p.253 no.1365). From this hadith...
Source pages p. 242

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with proof. It states that if the master declares 'This is my son' or 'This is my master' (i.e., the master calls the slave his master) or calls him 'O my master', the slave becomes free. The commentary explains the condition: the slave must be of an age that he could plausibly be the master's son (or the master's master in the other case), and the master must be serious, not joking. The reasoning is that by acknowledging paternity, the lineage of the slave is established with the master. Since the slave's lineage was unknown, once it becomes known as the master's son, the rule 'Whoever owns a close relative (dha rahim mahram) is freed' applies. The father-son relationship is a mahram relationship, so the slave son becomes free. The hadith of Samurah ibn Jundub is cited as proof: the Prophet said that if a person owns a close relative who is a mahram (such as a son or brother), that relative becomes free. Thus, the acknowledgment of paternity leads to freedom through this principle. The passage ends abruptly with 'from this hadith...' but the conclusion is clear.

Key Terms
ذو رحم محرم (dhu rahim muhram): A blood relative with whom marriage is permanently prohibited, such as son, father, brother.
من ملک ذا رحم محرم عتق (man malaka dha rahim muhram 'ataqa): Legal maxim: Whoever owns a mahram relative becomes free. Used as a basis for emancipation in certain cases.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) The slave becomes free
B) The slave remains a slave because it is just a statement
C) The slave becomes free only if the master also intends emancipation
D) The slave becomes free only if he is actually the master's son

Key Takeaway
A master's serious acknowledgment of a slave as his son or master, when the slave's lineage is unknown and the slave's age is plausible, triggers emancipation under the Hanafi school.

Kinayah in 'Itaq: Intention Required and 'La Sultan' Ineffective
Ruling with proof: kinayah in emancipation

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi rule that kinayah in emancipation requires intention and identify why the expression 'la sultan li 'alayk' does not free the slave even with intention.

Core Idea
For kinayah, intention is necessary but not sufficient; the expression must be capable of indicating emancipation.

This lesson examines the Hanafi rules for allusive expressions (kinayah) in declaring emancipation. While intention is generally required to give effect to a kinayah, some expressions are so ambiguous that they cannot effect emancipation even with explicit intent. The passage explains the principle and provides the hadith evidence from the case of Rukanah, then applies it to the phrase 'la sultan li 'alayk' (I have no authority over you).

Guided Path
1
Identify the general rule for kinayah in 'itaq
Read the opening lines: 'if he did not intend, he does not become free'. State that all kinayah words require intention for emancipation to occur.
This is analogous to kinayah in divorce.
2
Examine the specific expression 'la sultan li 'alayk'
Analyze why this phrase is ineffective: it can mean 'I have no authority' due to sale or the slave being a mukatib, so it does not necessarily imply emancipation.
The passage explicitly says it does not free even with intention.
3
Analyze the hadith proof
The hadith of Rukanah: the Prophet asked about intention when a kinayah was used for divorce. This establishes that intention is required for kinayah.
The hadith is ahad but accepted as evidence.
4
Trace the analogy from talaq to 'itaq
Understand that the rule for kinayah in 'itaq is derived by analogy from talaq, as both are acts of dissolution that can be expressed ambiguously.
The Hanafi school applies the same principle to both.
5
Apply the reasoning to distinguish effective and ineffective kinayah
For a kinayah to be effective, it must be capable of being interpreted as emancipation. 'La sultan' fails because it has other plausible meanings.
Other phrases like 'You are free' are sarih and need no intention.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كتاب العتاق
وان لم ينو لم يعتق وكذلك جميع كنايات العتق (۱۹ (۵) وان قال لا سلطان لي عليه ونوى به العتق لم يعتق
کنایہ کے الفاظ کے دو معنی ہوتے ہیں۔ ایک معنی سے آزادگی ہوگی اور دوسرے معنی لینے سے آزادگی نہیں ہوگی اس لئے آزاد کرنے کے لئے نیت کرنا ہو گا۔ مثلا میری تم پر ملکیت نہیں ہے کا ایک معنی یہ ہے کہ تم کو بیچ دیا اس لئے میری تم پر ملکیت نہیں ہے۔ اس سے آزادگی نہیں ہوگی۔ اور دوسرا معنی ہے کہ میں نے تم کو آزاد کر دیا اس لئے میری تم پر ملکیت نہیں ہے۔ اس لئے اس معنی لینے سے آزاد ہو جائے گا۔ لیکن اس معنی لینے کے لئے نیت کرنی ہوگی (۲) حضرت رکانہ نے بیوی کو طلاق دینے کے لئے لفظ کنایہ استعمال کیا تھا تو حضور نے اس کی نیت پوچھی ۔ پھر انہوں نے کہا کہ ایک طلاق کی نیت کی ہے اس لئے ایک طلاق واقع کی۔ حدیث یہ ہے۔ عن عبد الله بن على بن يزيد ركانة عن ابيه عن جده انه طلق امرأته البتة فاتى رسول الله الله الله فقال ما اردت؟ قال واحدة قال الله؟ قال الله! قال هو على ما اردت (الف) (ابوداؤد شریف، باب فی البیتی ص ۳۰۷ نمبر ۲۲۰۸، ترمذی شریف ، باب ما جاء فی الرجل يطلق امرأته البيتة ص ۲۲۲ نمبر ۱۱۷۷) اس حدیث میں البتہ کا لفظ کنایہ استعمال کیا ہے اس لئے آپ نے رکانہ سے نیت پوچھی۔
اصول تمام کنائی الفاظ میں یہی اصول ہے کہ نیت کرے گا تو آزادگی واقع ہوگی ورنہ نہیں۔ ۲۱۹۴] (۵) اور اگر کہا میرا تم پر غلبہ نہیں ہے اور اس سے آزادگی کی نیت کی تو آزاد نہیں ہوگا۔
میرا تم پر غلبہ نہیں ہے اس سے غلام آزاد ہونا ضروری نہیں ہے۔ کیونکہ مکاتب غلام پر ملکیت ہوتی ہے وہ آزاد نہیں ہوتا لیکن پھر بھی آقا کا اس پر غلبہ نہیں ہوتا۔ کیونکہ وہ تجارت کرنے اور اپنا نفقہ جمع کرنے میں آزاد ہوتا ہے۔ اس لئے اس جملے سے آزاد ہونا متیقن نہیں ہے۔ اس لئے اس جملے میں نیت کرنے سے بھی آزادگی واقع نہیں ہوگی۔
لاسلطان لی علیک : میرا تم پر غلبہ نہیں ہے۔
Book of Emancipation. And if he did not intend, he does not become free, and similarly all kinayah of emancipation. (19 (5) And if he said 'I have no authority over him' and intended emancipation by it, he does not become free. Kinayah words have two meanings. From one meaning freedom occurs, and from the other meaning freedom does not occur; therefore, for emancipation, intention is necessary. For example, 'I have no ownership over you' has one meaning: I have sold you, therefore I have no ownership over you. From this, freedom does not occur. And the other meaning is: I have freed you, therefore I have no ownership over you. Therefore, by taking this meaning, he becomes free. But to take this meaning, intention is necessary. (2) Sayyiduna Rukanah used a kinayah word for divorcing his wife, so the Prophet asked about his intention. Then he said that he intended one divorce, so one divorce occurred. The hadith is: From Abdullah ibn Ali ibn Yazid Rukanah from his father from his grandfather that he gave his wife a 'battah' divorce, then came to the Messenger of Allah, and he said: What did you intend? He said: One. He said: By Allah? He said: By Allah! He said: It is as you intended. (Abu Dawud, Tirmidhi) In this hadith, the word 'battah' is a kinayah, so the Prophet asked Rukanah about his intention. The principle for all kinayah words is that if he intends, emancipation occurs, otherwise not. [2194] (5) And if he said 'I have no domination over you' and intended emancipation by it, he does not become free. 'I have no domination over you' does not necessarily free the slave, because a mukatib slave is owned but not dominated, as he is free in trading and earning his sustenance. Therefore, emancipation from this phrase is not certain. Thus, even with intention, emancipation does not occur. 'La sultan li 'alayk': I have no domination over you.
Source pages p. 242

Commentary
This passage from Sharh al-Sameri discusses the use of kinayah (allusive expressions) in declaring emancipation. It first states the general rule: if the master does not intend freedom when using a kinayah, the slave is not freed. Similarly, all kinayah expressions require intention. Then it gives an example: if the master says 'I have no authority over you' and intends emancipation, it is not effective because the phrase can also mean 'I have sold you' etc. The commentary provides the reasoning: such phrases have multiple meanings, and intention alone is insufficient to establish emancipation. The hadith of Rukanah is cited as evidence that the Prophet asked about intention in a case of divorce using a kinayah. This principle is applied analogically to emancipation. The passage also specifically addresses the phrase 'la sultan li 'alayk' (no authority over you), explaining that it does not imply emancipation because a mukatib slave has no authority over him but is not free. Therefore, even with intention, it is not effective.

Key Terms
كناية (kinayah): An allusive expression that can mean emancipation or another meaning; requires intention for the intended effect.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) The slave is freed because intention is present.
B) The slave is not freed because the phrase does not clearly indicate emancipation.
C) The slave is freed only if he is a mukatib.
D) The slave is freed if the master repeats it three times.

Key Takeaway
For kinayah in Hanafi 'itaq, intention is necessary, but only for expressions that can bear the meaning of freedom; 'la sultan li 'alayk' does not free even with intention.

Kinayah Expressions and Emancipation: From 'Hadha Ibni' to 'Ya Akhi'
Rulings on specific kinayah expressions for emancipation

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to classify kinayah expressions as either effecting emancipation or not, and apply the principle that intention is required for ambiguous words.

Core Idea
Calling a slave 'my son' (hadha ibni) may free him if paternity is established; 'my master' (mawla) frees him; but 'my brother' (ya akhi) is only a term of respect.

This passage from a Hanafi fiqh text addresses a subtle area of emancipation law: when a master uses ambiguous kinayah expressions. The ruling distinguishes between words that imply a change of status and those that are merely terms of endearment. Understanding this helps us see the importance of intention (niyyah) in Islamic legal acts.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two groups in the ruling
The text lists expressions that free (hadha ibni, hadha mawla, ya mawlay) and those that don't (ya bni, ya akhi).
Note the difference in form: 'hadha ibni' (this is my son) vs 'ya bni' (O my son).
2
Understand the basis for 'hadha ibni'
This frees only if the slave is of unknown lineage and young enough to be the master's son, establishing paternity.
The commentary mentions that paternity leads to freedom because one cannot own a mahram relative.
3
Understand the basis for 'hadha mawla' and 'ya mawlay'
The word 'mawla' can mean freed slave; since the master owns the slave, calling him 'mawla' means 'my freed one', thus emancipation.
'Ya mawlay' is the vocative form and similarly indicates emancipation.
4
Understand why 'ya bni' and 'ya akhi' do not free
These are conventional terms of endearment or respect, not legal declarations of paternity or brotherhood.
The commentary explains that 'ya akhi' is akin to addressing someone as 'brother' out of affection, not establishing actual brotherhood.
5
Note the general principle
All kinayah expressions require intention to effect emancipation, as per the hadith of Rukanah about divorce.
The hadith illustrates that the Prophet (saw) asked Rukanah about his intention when he used a kinayah word for divorce.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
[۲۱۹۵] (۲) واذا قال هذا ابنى وثبت على ذلك او قال هذا مولاي او يا مولاي عتق [۲۱۹۶] (۷) وان قال يا بنى او يا اخي لم يعتق
کنایہ کے الفاظ کے دو معنی ہوتے ہیں۔ ایک معنی سے آزادگی ہوگی اور دوسرے معنی لینے سے آزادگی نہیں ہوگی اس لئے آزاد کرنے کے لئے نیت کرنا ہو گا۔ مثلا میری تم پر ملکیت نہیں ہے کا ایک معنی یہ ہے کہ تم کو بیچ دیا اس لئے میری تم پر ملکیت نہیں ہے۔ اس سے آزادگی نہیں ہوگی۔ اور دوسرا معنی ہے کہ میں نے تم کو آزاد کر دیا اس لئے میری تم پر ملکیت نہیں ہے۔ اس لئے اس معنی لینے سے آزاد ہو جائے گا۔ لیکن اس معنی لینے کے لئے نیت کرنی ہوگی (۲) حضرت رکانہ نے بیوی کو طلاق دینے کے لئے لفظ کنایہ استعمال کیا تھا تو حضور نے اس کی نیت پوچھی ۔ پھر انہوں نے کہا کہ ایک طلاق کی نیت کی ہے اس لئے ایک طلاق واقع کی۔ حدیث یہ ہے۔ عن عبد الله بن على بن يزيد ركانة عن ابيه عن جده انه طلق امرأته البتة فاتى رسول الله الله الله فقال ما اردت؟ قال واحدة قال الله؟ قال الله! قال هو على ما اردت (الف) (ابوداؤد شریف، باب فی البیتی ص ۳۰۷ نمبر ۲۲۰۸، ترمذی شریف ، باب ما جاء فی الرجل يطلق امرأته البيتة ص ۲۲۲ نمبر ۱۱۷۷) اس حدیث میں البتہ کا لفظ کنایہ استعمال کیا ہے اس لئے آپ نے رکانہ سے نیت پوچھی۔
اصول تمام کنائی الفاظ میں یہی اصول ہے کہ نیت کرے گا تو آزادگی واقع ہوگی ورنہ نہیں۔ ۲۱۹۴] (۵) اور اگر کہا میرا تم پر غلبہ نہیں ہے اور اس سے آزادگی کی نیت کی تو آزاد نہیں ہوگا۔
میرا تم پر غلبہ نہیں ہے اس سے غلام آزاد ہونا ضروری نہیں ہے۔ کیونکہ مکاتب غلام پر ملکیت ہوتی ہے وہ آزاد نہیں ہوتا لیکن پھر بھی آقا کا اس پر غلبہ نہیں ہوتا۔ کیونکہ وہ تجارت کرنے اور اپنا نفقہ جمع کرنے میں آزاد ہوتا ہے۔ اس لئے اس جملے سے آزاد ہونا متیقن نہیں ہے۔ اس لئے اس جملے میں نیت کرنے سے بھی آزادگی واقع نہیں ہوگی۔
لاسلطان لی علیک : میرا تم پر غلبہ نہیں ہے۔
[2195] (2) And if he said 'this is my son' and persists on that, or said 'this is my master' or 'O my master', he becomes free. [2196] (7) And if he said 'O my son' or 'O my brother', he does not become free. Commentary: ...
Source pages p. 242

Commentary
This passage presents two groups of kinayah expressions. The first group (hadha ibni, hadha mawla, ya mawlay) effect emancipation when stated with the appropriate context. The second group (ya bni, ya akhi) do not free the slave because they are used affectionately or respectfully and do not clearly indicate emancipation. The commentary explains that for 'hadha ibni' to free, the slave must be young enough to be the master's son and his lineage unknown, so that paternity can be established, leading to freedom by the rule that one who owns a mahram relative is freed. For 'hadha mawla' and 'ya mawlay', the word 'mawla' has two meanings: master or freed slave; since the slave is already owned, the intended meaning is 'my freed slave', thus emancipation occurs. For 'ya bni' and 'ya akhi', these are expressions of endearment and do not effect emancipation.

Key Terms
مولى (mawla): A word with dual meaning: master (owner) or freed slave; in context of 'hadha mawla' or 'ya mawlay', it means freed slave.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If a master says to his slave 'O my brother' (ya akhi), what is the ruling?
A) The slave is freed because it shows affection.
B) The slave is not freed because it is a respectful address, not a declaration of freedom.
C) The slave is freed if the master also intends emancipation.
D) The slave becomes free only if he is related by blood.

Key Takeaway
Kinayah expressions that imply a change of status (like mawla) effect emancipation, while terms of endearment (like ya akhi) do not, and intention is key for ambiguous words.

Intra-Hanafi Dispute on Metaphorical Sonship and Emancipation
Intra-Hanafi dispute on metaphorical son and emancipation

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the difference between Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn regarding metaphorical declarations of paternity and state the rationale for each position.

Core Idea
When a slave cannot be the master's son, Abu Hanifa frees him via metaphor; Sahibayn do not.

This lesson examines an intra-Hanafi dispute about the effect of a master saying 'this is my son' to a slave who is so similar in age that biological paternity is impossible. The key question is whether such a statement can be interpreted metaphorically to mean emancipation. Imam Abu Hanifa accepts the metaphorical interpretation, while the Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani) consider the statement meaningless.

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi position (Abu Hanifa)
Identify the ruling according to Imam Abu Hanifa: the slave is freed because the word 'son' is interpreted metaphorically to mean 'freed slave'.
This relies on the principle of majaz (metaphorical usage) in legal language.
2
State the Sahibayn position
Identify the ruling according to Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani: the slave is not freed because biological impossibility prevents any literal or metaphorical paternity, making the statement futile.
They require the possibility of literal meaning for the statement to have legal effect.
3
Analyze the proofs
Abu Hanifa relies on the master's intention and the customary use of 'son' to mean 'freed slave' (majaz). The Sahibayn argue that since paternity cannot be established, the statement is void and cannot effect emancipation.
The dispute is essentially about whether legal meaning can override literal impossibility.
4
Determine the practical consequence (thamarah al-khilaf)
If a master says 'this is my son' to a slave who is his age, Abu Hanifa frees the slave; the Sahibayn do not. Thus the fatwa depends on which view is adopted.
The text does not specify which view is mufta bihi, so both are recorded.

Source Text

[۲۱۹۷] (۸) وان قال الغلام لا يولد مثله لمثله هذا ابنى عتق عليه عند ابي حنيفة رحمه الله و عندهما لا يعتق
معلوم ہوا کہ ذی رحم محرم کا مالک ہوا تو وہ آزاد ہو جائے گا۔
اور میرے مولی کے دو معنی ہیں۔ ایک معنی ہے میرا آقا اور دوسرا معنی ہے میرا آزاد کیا ہو اغلام ۔ اور یہ چونکہ پہلے سے غلام ہے اس لئے میرے آقا کا معنی نہیں لے سکتے۔ اس لئے میرا آزاد کردہ غلام والا معنی متعین ہے۔ اس لئے اس لفظ سے آزاد ہو جائے گا۔ اور اے میرے مولی ! کے بھی یہی دو معنی ہیں ۔ اور اس میں بھی میرا آزاد کردہ غلام والا معنی لیا جائے گا اور غلام آزاد ہو جائے گا۔ فرق صرف اتنا ہے کہ اس میں ندا کے ساتھ کہا اے میرے مولی! اور پہلے میں اشارہ کر کے کہا کہ یہ میرا مولی ہے۔ ۲۱۹۶] (۷) اور اگر کہا اے میرے بیٹے یا اے میرے بھائی تو آزاد نہ ہوگا۔ یہ الفاظ پیار کے طور پر کہتے ہیں۔ اور کبھی احترام کے لئے بھی کہتے ہیں۔ اس لئے ان سے آزاد کر نا متعین نہیں ہوا۔ اس لئے اے میرے بیٹے ، یا اے میرے بھائی کہنے سے غلام آزاد نہیں ہوگا۔ ۲۱۹۷] (۸) اگر ایسے غلام کے بارے میں کہا جو اس جیسا لڑکا اس جیسے سے پیدا نہیں ہو سکتا کہ یہ میرا بیٹا ہے تو امام ابوحنیفہ کے نزدیک آزاد ہو جائے گا اور صاحبین کے نزدیک آزاد نہیں ہوگا ۔ ا غلام آقا کے ہم عمر تھا اس جیسا غلام اس عمر کے آقا کی اولاد نہیں ہوسکتی تھی۔ ایسے غلام کے بارے میں آقانے کہا کہ یہ میرابیٹا ہے تو امام ابو حنیفہ کے نزدیک وہ غلام آزاد ہو جائے گا۔ اس صورت میں عمر بڑی ہونے کی وجہ سے غلام حقیقت میں بیٹا تو نہیں بن پائے گا البتہ مجاز پر مل کیا جائے گا۔ اور یوں کہا جائے گا کہ بیٹا بول کر آزادگی مراد لی ہے۔ جس کی وجہ سے غلام آزاد ہو جائے گا۔
صاحبین فرماتے ہیں کہ حقیقت میں تو بیٹا بن نہیں سکتا اس لئے آقا سے نسب ثابت نہیں ہوگا۔ اور جب بیٹا نہیں بن سکتا تو آزاد بھی نہیں ہوگا ۔ اور کلام کو لغو قرار دیا جائے گا۔
[2197] (8) And if he said about a slave who cannot be born like him to someone like him, 'this is my son', it is freed according to Abu Hanifa, may Allah have mercy on him, but according to the two (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad), it is not freed. Commentary: ...
Source pages p. 243

Commentary
This passage presents an intra-Hanafi dispute regarding a slave who is so similar in age to the master that it is biologically impossible for him to be the master's son. Imam Abu Hanifa rules that the slave is freed because the master's statement is interpreted as a metaphorical declaration of emancipation; the word 'son' is used figuratively to indicate freedom. The Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani) argue that since paternity cannot be established in reality, the statement is meaningless and does not effect emancipation. The relied-upon position (mufta bihi) is not explicitly stated here, but the text presents both views.

Key Terms
مجاز (majaz): Metaphorical usage; here, 'son' is used metaphorically to mean 'freed slave'.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Sahibayn, if a master says to a slave who is his age and cannot be his son 'this is my son', what is the ruling?
A) The slave is freed because the master intended freedom.
B) The slave is not freed because the statement is impossible and thus meaningless.
C) The slave is freed only if he is younger than the master.
D) The slave becomes free after the master's death.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, a master's statement 'this is my son' to a biologically impossible slave leads to a legitimate ikhtilaf: Abu Hanifa frees, Sahibayn do not.

Ruling on Saying 'Anti Taliq' to a Slave Woman with Intention of Emancipation
Explanation that divorce terminology cannot be used for emancipation

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling when a master says 'anti taliq' to his slave woman with intent to emancipate, and explain the legal principle that a weaker term cannot convey a stronger meaning.

Core Idea
Divorce terminology is too weak to effect emancipation—part cannot substitute for the whole.

In this lesson, we examine a specific case: a master says 'anti taliq' (you are divorced) to his female slave, intending to free her. The ruling, based on a key linguistic-legal principle, is that she does not become free. We will unpack the reasoning behind this ruling and its implications for legal declarations.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and intention
The act is the master's statement 'anti taliq' directed at his female slave. His intention is to free her (emancipation).
Intention alone does not change the legal effect of the words.
2
State the ruling
The Hanafi ruling is that the slave woman is not freed. The statement is ineffective for emancipation.
The ruling is clear: no freedom results.
3
Understand the legal reasoning
Divorce (talaq) terminates only partial ownership (milk al-bud'), while emancipation (itaq) terminates full ownership. Using a term that covers only a part to achieve the whole is invalid.
The key distinction is between partial and full ownership.
4
Apply the underlying principle
The principle is that a weaker (da'if) term cannot be used to intend a stronger (qawi) meaning. This is not a recognized metaphorical usage (majaz muta'arif).
The principle safeguards legal certainty.
5
Note the consequence
Even with a clear intention, the declaration fails. The master must use proper emancipation wording (e.g., 'anta hurrun') to free the slave.
This prevents ambiguity in legal rights.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
[۲۱۹۸](۹) وان قال لامته انت طالق ونوى به الحرية لم تعتق.
(۲۱۹۸] (۹) اور اگر اپنی باندی سے کہا کہ تجھے طلاق ہے اور اس سے آزادگی کی نیت کی تو آزاد نہیں ہوگی۔ نکاح میں صرف ملک بضعہ ہوتی ہے جو جزوی ملک ہے ۔ اور طلاق کے ذریعہ اس ملک کو ختم کرنا ہے اور عتاق کے ذریعہ پورے جسم کی ملکیت ختم کرتے ہیں جو کل ہے اس لئے جز یعنی طلاق بول کر کل یعنی عتاق مراد لینا مشکل ہے۔ کیونکہ ضعیف بول کر قومی مراد لینا مجاز متعارف نہیں ہے۔ اس لئے انت طالق بول کر عتاق مراد لینا جائز نہیں ہوگا۔
اص یہ مسئلہ اس اصول پر ہے کہ ضعیف لفظ بول کر قومی لفظ مراد لینا صحیح نہیں ہے کیونکہ وہ کمزور ہے۔
[2198] (9) And if he said to his slave woman 'you are divorced' and intended freedom by it, she does not become free. Commentary: In marriage, only ownership of the vulva (milk al-bud') exists, which is partial ownership. Through divorce, this ownership is terminated, while through emancipation, ownership of the entire body is terminated. Therefore, using the part (divorce) to mean the whole (emancipation) is problematic because using a weaker term to mean a stronger one is not a common metaphor. Hence, using 'anti taliq' to mean emancipation is not permissible. The principle is that it is not correct to use a weaker word to mean a stronger one, because it is weak.
Source pages p. 243

Commentary
This passage addresses the ruling when a master says to his female slave 'you are divorced' (anti taliq) with the intention of freeing her. The text rules that she is not freed. The reasoning is that 'taliq' (divorce) relates to termination of marriage rights (milk al-bud'), which is a partial ownership, while 'itaq' (emancipation) terminates full ownership of the slave's entire body. Using a specific term (part) to indicate a general concept (whole) is not a recognized metaphorical usage in the Arabic language. Therefore, the statement does not effect emancipation, even with intention. The underlying legal principle is that a weaker expression cannot be used to convey a stronger meaning.

Key Terms
ضعیف (da'if): Weak; here referring to a term that is more specific or limited in scope (divorce) compared to the intended general meaning (emancipation).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If a master says to his female slave 'you are divorced' (anti taliq) intending to free her, what is the ruling?
A) She is freed because intention determines the meaning.
B) She is not freed because divorce is a weaker term not applicable to emancipation.
C) She is freed only if she is also his wife.
D) She becomes free after the master's death.

Key Takeaway
A weaker legal term cannot be used to effect a stronger legal consequence, even with intention.

Impossible Lineage Claim: Emancipation via Majaz
Intra-Hanafi dispute on emancipation via impossible lineage claim

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi positions on the legal effect of an impossible lineage claim and explain the usul principle of majaz that supports Abu Hanifa's ruling.

Core Idea
When literal truth is impossible, Hanafi usul rescues the statement via metaphorical interpretation.

This passage presents a dispute within the Hanafi school: a master claims a slave of the same age as his son. The literal meaning is impossible, so how should the law treat the statement? Abu Hanifa applies the principle of majaz (metaphor) to give effect to the likely intention of emancipation, while the Sahibayn deem the statement futile (laghw). Understanding this case illuminates how Hanafi usul balances textual fidelity with practical legal consequences.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and the dispute
The act is a master's statement 'This is my son' about a slave who cannot biologically be his son. The dispute is whether this statement has legal effect (emancipation) or is disregarded.
2
Understand the impossibility condition
The slave is the same age or older than the master, making biological fatherhood impossible. This triggers the question of how to interpret an impossible literal statement.
3
Abu Hanifa's position and its basis in majaz
Since the literal meaning is impossible, the statement is interpreted metaphorically. The only plausible legal intention is emancipation, so the slave is freed.
Key principle: when haqiqah (literal) fails, majaz (metaphor) is used to salvage meaning.
4
Sahibayn's position and its basis in laghw
They argue that because the statement is factually impossible, it is simply false and carries no legal weight. The speech is considered laghw (futile).
Contrast: they prioritize literal truth over inferred intention.
5
Determine the mufta bihi and rationale
The relied-upon (mufta bihi) position in the Hanafi school is Abu Hanifa's view, because the legal system strives to give effect to speech where possible, and majaz is a well-established tool.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
[۲۱۹۷] (۸) اگر ایسے غلام کے بارے میں کہا جو اس جیسا لڑکا اس جیسے سے پیدا نہیں ہو سکتا کہ یہ میرا بیٹا ہے تو امام ابوحنیفہ کے نزدیک آزاد ہو جائے گا اور صاحبین کے نزدیک آزاد نہیں ہوگا ۔ ا غلام آقا کے ہم عمر تھا اس جیسا غلام اس عمر کے آقا کی اولاد نہیں ہوسکتی تھی۔ ایسے غلام کے بارے میں آقانے کہا کہ یہ میرابیٹا ہے تو امام ابو حنیفہ کے نزدیک وہ غلام آزاد ہو جائے گا۔ اس صورت میں عمر بڑی ہونے کی وجہ سے غلام حقیقت میں بیٹا تو نہیں بن پائے گا البتہ مجاز پر مل کیا جائے گا۔ اور یوں کہا جائے گا کہ بیٹا بول کر آزادگی مراد لی ہے۔ جس کی وجہ سے غلام آزاد ہو جائے گا۔
صاحبین فرماتے ہیں کہ حقیقت میں تو بیٹا بن نہیں سکتا اس لئے آقا سے نسب ثابت نہیں ہوگا۔ اور جب بیٹا نہیں بن سکتا تو آزاد بھی نہیں ہوگا ۔ اور کلام کو لغو قرار دیا جائے گا۔
[2197] (8) If he says regarding a slave who is such that a boy like him cannot be born from someone like him, 'This is my son', then according to Abu Hanifa mercy be upon him the slave is emancipated, while according to the two (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) he is not emancipated. The slave was of the same age as the master; a similar slave could not be the offspring of a master of that age. In such a case, the master says 'This is my son'. According to Imam Abu Hanifa, the slave will be freed. In this situation, due to advanced age, the slave cannot be a son in reality, but it will be interpreted metaphorically. It will be said that by saying 'son' he meant emancipation, so the slave becomes free. The two (Sahibayn) say that in reality he cannot be a son, so lineage from the master is not established. And since he cannot be a son, he will not be freed either. The speech will be considered futile.
Source pages p. 243

Commentary
This is an intra-Hanafi dispute regarding a master who says 'This is my son' about a slave who is clearly too old to be his biological son. Imam Abu Hanifa argues that since the literal truth is impossible, the statement must be interpreted metaphorically to give it legal effect. The only plausible intention is emancipation, so the slave is freed. The Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani) argue that because the statement is factually impossible, it is simply false and invalid, so no legal consequences follow. The slave remains a slave. This case illustrates the Hanafi usul principle of prioritizing the literal meaning, and when that is impossible, resorting to metaphorical interpretation if a legally valid meaning can be assigned.

Key Terms
مجاز (majaz): Metaphorical or figurative interpretation of a statement when literal meaning is impossible.
لغو (laghw): Futile or meaningless speech that carries no legal consequence.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the basis for Abu Hanifa's ruling in the case of 'This is my son' when the slave cannot be his son?
A) The statement is taken as a joke
B) The statement is interpreted metaphorically to mean emancipation
C) The master's lineage is established
D) The slave is considered a mahram

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, an impossible literal statement is not automatically futile; if a valid intention can be inferred via majaz, the statement is given legal effect—as when a master says 'This is my son' of an older slave, the slave is freed.
